==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The aim of this thesis has been to study the NOx reducing properties of am-
monium sulfate, in order to evaluate the use of it as an alternative additive in the
SNCR process.
Initially, a laboratory-scale experimental setup capable of feeding micron-sized
droplets to a homogeneous ﬂow reactor, in which experiments were performed, has
been developed. Subsequently, the NO reducing properties of ammonium sulfate,
as an additive in the SNCR process, have been experimentally investigated. The
experiments were designed to investigate the eﬀect of varying oxygen levels (1.25%−
8.19%) and initial AS/NO ratios (0.38−1.98) in a temperature range of ∼973−1173
K. Furthermore, the reduction using ammonium sulfate has been compared to the
reduction of reference gas experiments using ammonia gas as reducing agent, both
in the presence and absence of SO2. The experimental results have been compared
to modelling results of an initial chemical kinetics model and a modiﬁed chemical
kinetics model.
The NO reductions obtained during the experiments of this project using ammo-
nium sulfate were relative low, with NOﬁnal/NOinitial = 0.47 for the best reduction,
relative to reductions of down to NOﬁnal/NOinitial ≈0.06 from the literature using
ammonia as reducing agent. The NO reduction is initiated at lower temperatures
of ∼950 −1000 K in this projects, relative to ∼1050 −1070 K for ammonia in
the literature using similar reactors. Furthermore, the optimum temperature during
the experiments has been found to be between 1031 −1144 K, while the optimum
temperature varies between ∼1170 −1240 K in the literature. However, the down-
ward shift of the temperature window may be caused by high residence time in the
reactor during the experiments of this project.
For increasing initial ratio of AS/NO, it was found that the optimum temper-
ature of reduction was not signiﬁcantly changed. However, the NO reduction ef-
ﬁciency increases by an increase in the initial ratio of AS/NO. By lowering of the
oxygen concentration in the gas system, the maximum reduction of NO is improved,
while the temperature window is shifted towards higher temperatures.
From gas reference experiments, it was found that SO2 has an inhibiting eﬀect
on NO reduction at the conditions studied during this project.
Ammonium sulfate is signiﬁcantly less eﬀective as a reducing agent compared to
ammonia gas.
The modelling results of the project signiﬁcantly overestimates the NO reduction
relative to the observed reduction from experiments. The model modiﬁcations made
for the ﬁnal model did not improve the performance of the model compared to the
experimental results.

nical University of Denmark. The project has been written in the period from January

Project description
Project title: Selective non-catalytic reduction of nitric oxide by ammonium sulfate
Author: Martin Pilgaard Jensen, s134455
ECTS points: 32.5
Supervisors: Peter Glarborg, Hao Wu, Hamid Hashemi, Kristian Røhe Kongsted Krum
Technician: Anders Kjersgaard
Background
The handling of municipal solid waste (MSW), which is produced in large amounts on a
daily basis, has become a signiﬁcant challenge. The traditional handling method, where
the MSW is disposed at landﬁlls is in many regions no longer possible, due to signiﬁcant
environmental concerns and a shortage of landﬁll sites. An alternative to landﬁlling is
extraction of energy through combustion in waste-to-energy (WtE) plants. The main
technology for combustion of MSW is grate-ﬁring. This technology enables the use of a
wide range of fuels with varying composition and moisture content, which is very conve-
nient for combustion of MSW due to the heterogeneous nature of the fuel. An additional
driver for the growing interest in WtE is the expected increase of the energy demand of
the world and the decrease of known reserves of fossil fuel feedstocks. WtE technology
has the potential to replace part of the fossil fuel consumption of the world by MSW.
MSW is composed of many diﬀerent waste fractions, including food waste, yard waste,
paper, board, plastics, metals, glass ware, wood, textiles, rubber, leather and more. Most
of the waste fractions in MSW are nitrogen containing materials, and thus form nitrogen
oxides (NOx) during combustion. The emission of NOx has been, and continuous to be
a major environmental concern, as NOx participates in the formation of acid rain and
photochemical smog. Stringent limits on NOx emissions force the industry to take control
measures to reduce NOx emissions from the WtE plants. At the same time, the waste
fractions also contains signiﬁcant amounts of inorganic elements and heavy metals, in
particular sodium, potassium, lead and zink. These elements bind easily with chloride,
which is also found in high concentrations in the waste fractions. Some alkali and heavy
metal chlorides, including sodium, potassium, lead and zink chlorides, are known from
the industry to be easily volatilized, and can cause severe corrosion and deposition in the
boiler section of WtE plants.
A number of diﬀerent control techniques for the reduction of NOx has already been devel-
oped and integrated in the industry. In Denmark, the most common technique to reduce
NOx emissions from grate-ﬁred WtE plants is selective non-catalytic reduction (SNCR)
using ammonia as the reducing agent. It has recently been discovered that ammonium
sulfate, which can be thermally decomposed into ammonia and SO2/SO3, is promising as
an alternative additive in the SNCR process for the combined reduction of NOx emissions,
deposition and corrosion.
v
==PAGE:8==
Objectives and approaches
The objective of this project is to study and evaluate the use of ammonium sulfate as
an alternative additive in the SNCR process, for the simultaneous reduction of NOx,
corrosion and deposition. Thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate will be investi-
gated experimentally over a wide range of temperatures, in order to gain insight in the
decomposition behavior and gas phase products.
As part of this project, a laboratory-scale setup will be developed, which can feed liquid
droplets of ammonium sulfate into an SNCR system. It is desired to use this experimental
setup to determine the gas phase products at varying conditions, in order to gain insight
on the SNCR performance when using ammonium sulfate as the reducing agent. Further-
more, it is desired to model the kinetics of the SNCR process using ammonia sulfate as
the reducing agent.
Content
• A literature review.
• An experimental investigation on the thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate.
• Development of a laboratory-scale setup, which can feed liquid droplets of ammo-
nium sulfate into an SNCR system.
• An experimental investigation on the SNCR performance using ammonium sulfate
as the reducing agent, including modelling of the kinetics of the process.
Time schedule
Project start - 08.01.2018
Project description - 18.02.2018
First progress report: Literature review - 23.02.2018
Second progress report: Experimental and procedures - 30.03.2018
Third progress report: Results and discussion - 01.06.2018
Final report: 24.06.2018

1
Introduction
The traditional handling method of municipal solid waste, MSW, has been to dispose
it at landﬁlls [1]. However, in order to relieve the problem of signiﬁcant environmental
concerns and shortage of landﬁll sites, the MSW must be handled in a more sustainable
manner. These concerns can be relieved by waste incineration. Furthermore, energy can
be extracted from combustion of waste, which will be used to cover the world’s increasing
energy demand.
However, as waste incineration is a combustion process, nitrogen oxides (NOx) are formed
during combustion [2]. Stringent regulations have been put into use in order to reduce the
emissions of NOx, as it is an environmental problem. Nitric oxide and nitrogen dioxide
are precursors of acid rain, and NOx participate in the generation of photochemical smog
[3, 4, 5].
The reduction of NOx is most often achieved by Selective Catalytic Reduction (SCR) in
coal-ﬁred power plants. However, the performance of SCR catalysts can be signiﬁcantly
reduced due to poisoning and fouling of the catalysts by the high amount of heavy and
alkali metals released from waste incineration [1, 6, 7, 8].
Therefore, Selective Non-
Catalytic Reduction (SNCR) is more commonly used for NOx control in waste incineration
systems.
Deposition and corrosion on the surface of heat exchangers is another challenge related
to combustion of waste [6]. Especially corrosion is a challenge of waste incineration, due
to its high content of chlorine.
Recently, it has been discovered that ammonium sulfate, which can be thermally de-
composed into ammonia and SO2/SO3, is a promising alternative additive in the SNCR
process for the combined reduction of NOx emissions, deposition and corrosion.
During this project, the reducing properties of ammonium sulfate in SNCR have been
experimentally investigated in a laboratory-scale setup, developed during this project,
and compared to NH3, which is commonly used for SNCR. Furthermore, modelling work
has been performed and compared to the experimental results.
1
==PAGE:14==
==PAGE:15==
2
Literature review
2.1
Overview of waste combustion and related challenges
An alternative to the traditional handling method of municipal solid waste (MSW), where
it has been disposed at landﬁlls, is extraction of energy through combustion in waste-to-
energy (WtE) plants.
In this way the problem of signiﬁcant environmental concerns
and shortage of landﬁll sites can be relieved, while heat and power to cover the world’s
increasing energy demand is produced at the same time. Incineration of MSW can be
achieved using a variety of combustion systems, such as travelling grate, rotary kilns and
ﬂuidized beds [9]. However, for ﬂuidized bed technologies, some degree of pretreatment
and/or selective collection of waste is required in order to obtain a certain particle size
range. Thus, for the incineration of mixed municipal wastes, grate incinerators are widely
applied. In fact, grates are used for approximately 90% of the combustion systems treating
MSW in Europe [9].
Essentially all combustion processes form nitrogen oxides, which are collectively termed
NOx [3]. Most of the nitrogen oxides are released as NO, but also NO2 and N2O are
emitted. More than 95% of NOx emitted is in the form of NO, which is formed from one
of three mechanisms [2]:
• Thermal NO formation.
• Prompt NO formation.
• Fuel NO.
Thermal NO is formed at temperatures above 1773 K by oxidation of atmospheric molecu-
lar nitrogen [2]. However, in WtE processes, the formation of thermal NO can be regarded
as negligible due to the relatively low ﬂue gas temperatures, ranging from approximately
1023 −1373 K [10].
Prompt NO is formed at temperatures below 1073 K [2], through the rapid splitting of
the triple bond of the N2 molecules in the presence of hydrocarbon radicals. However,
for combustion of solid fuels like coal and waste, it is generally accepted that prompt NO
contributes insigniﬁcantly in the overall formation of NO [2, 3].
MSW have a high content of chemically-bound nitrogen [2]. This can be either in a form
of organic amines, amides, nitriles etc. or the nitrogen compounds NH3 and HCN. These
species are released from the fuel and via NHi as an intermediate, either NO or N2 is
formed depending on the combustion conditions. Therefore, in WtE, fuel NO is by far
the most dominant mechanism in the formation of NO.
The emission of NOx is an environmental problem, as nitric oxide and nitrogen dioxide
are precursors of acid rain [3, 4, 5]. Furthermore, NOx participate in the generation of
photochemical smog [3, 4, 5]. Therefore, it is desirable to reduce the emissions of NOx
and strict regulations have been put into use in order to achieve this goal.
3
==PAGE:16==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
In coal-ﬁred power plants, the reduction of NOx is most often achieved by Selective Cat-
alytic Reduction (SCR). This is a process where NOx is selectively reduced over a catalyst
surface, using a reducing agent [1, 11, 12]. Often the reducing agent is ammonia or urea.
The SCR process has a high eﬃciency, good selectivity and low cost. However, the ﬂue
gas composition from waste incineration plants is more complex relative to that from
coal-ﬁred plants [1]. The performance of SCR catalysts can be signiﬁcantly reduced due
to poisoning and fouling of the catalysts. This is caused by the high amount of heavy and
alkali metals as well as acid gases released from waste incineration [1, 6, 7, 8]. This is the
main reason why the SCR process has not been applied to the same extent as Selective
Non-Catalytic Reduction (SNCR) in the waste incineration industry. The SNCR process
will be described in section 2.2.
It has however become a topic of interest to overcome the problems of the complex and
unstable compositions in the ﬂue gases from MSW incineration plants, and to develop a
series of catalysts with high eﬃciency and good stability [1]. Recently, the SCR process has
been applied to some commercialized WtE plants. For example, it has been employed at
the Algonquin Power Energy-from-Waste Facility [13], in a tail-end position downstream
of the fabric ﬁlter baghouses. Due to that the SCR unit was placed downstream of fabric
ﬁlter baghouses, the problems with catalyst deactivation by alkali and heavy metal rich
particles in the ﬂue gas have been signiﬁcantly reduced. In order to remove particles
leaked from the fabric ﬁlter baghouses, the catalyst has been cleaned on a bi-yearly basis.
The system has been able to reduce the emission of NOx to a level below 110 ppm at
11% O2 with an ammonia slip of only 1.4 ppm. The largest disadvantage of the design is
the need for ﬂue gas reheating, in order to avoid deposition of ammonium sulfate on the
catalyst, which results in high annual operating costs [13].
Another challenge related to the combustion of waste is deposition and corrosion on heat
exchange surfaces [6]. Compared to other solid fules, municipal solid waste has a high
content of sulfur, chlorine, alkali metals and heavy metals like lead and zinc [1, 6, 7, 8].
Upon combustion, parts of these elements are volatilized and transported in the ﬂue gas,
forming deposits on evaporators, superheaters and economisers in the convective area of
the boiler. These low-melting point deposits are very corrosive [14].
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Selective non-catalytic reduction, also known as SNCR, is a post-combustion control pro-
cess for reduction of NOx [15, 16]. The employment of a post-combustion control technique
for reduction of NOx is necessary due to increasingly stringent legislation on nitrogen ox-
ide emissions [3]. Due to the strict legislation, primary measures of NOx reduction, such
as staged combustion, are inadequate.
In combustion of fuels such as biomass and waste, SNCR is commonly used for NOx
control, as catalytic cleaning is problematic due to catalyst poisoning [3]. The reducing
agents are injected into the hot ﬂue gasses, after which they react with the NOx [15, 16].
The most common reductants are ammonia, either in an aqueous solution or in gaseous
4
==PAGE:17==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
form, and urea in an aqueous solution. The overall post-combustion reactions proceed as
follows using ammonia and urea respectively [15, 17]:
4 NH3 + 4 NO + O2 −−→4 N2 + 6 H2O
(R 2.1)
NH2CONH2 + 2 NO + 1
2 O2 −−→2 N2 + CO2 + 2 H2O
(R 2.2)
Some of the important factors regarding SNCR performance are the operational temper-
ature, residence time, concentration of CO and O2, mixing of ﬂue gas and reducing agent
to NOx molar ratio [17]. The eﬀect of these factors will be reviewed in the following
subsections along with some other relevant factors. Urea decomposes rapidly to NH3 and
HNCO at high temperatures and the chemistry of the SNCR process using urea as a
reducing agent shares important similarities with the SCNR process using NH3 as a re-
ducing agent [3]. In the following subsections, studies using the Thermal DeNOx process,
which is SNCR with ammonia as reducing agent, have been used to investigate the eﬀect
of diﬀerent parameters on SNCR performance.
2.2.1
Dependence of SNCR operational temperature
The temperature window of operation is fairly narrow for SNCR, where eﬀective operation
is only achieved in the range of approximately 1100 −1400 K [2, 15, 16, 18, 19, 20, 21],
with the optimum being in the 1170 −1270 K region [15, 22, 23]. The NOx reducing
reaction is too slow to be signiﬁcant at temperatures below 1100 K, and above 1400 K
the reducing NH3 component is oxidized to NO rather than N2, causing a net production
of NO rather than the desired reduction.
Competition between consumption and production reactions of NO during the NH3 oxida-
tion determines the NO concentration. A simpliﬁed reaction scheme in the NO reduction
regime is constituted by [18, 24]:
NH3 + OH⇌NH2 + H2O
(R 2.3)
NH2 + NO⇌N2 + H + OH
(R 2.4)
NH2 + NO⇌N2 + H2O
(R 2.5)
H + O2⇌O + OH
(R 2.6)
O + H2O⇌OH + OH
(R 2.7)
The sequence of reactions is chain terminating at temperatures below a temperature of
approximately 1050 K, as the branching fraction of reactions R 2.4 and R 2.5 is low. This
results in little or no reaction, and consequently the concentrations of NO and NH3 remain
at their initial levels [24]. Thus, the NO is not reduced, while most or all NH3 is emitted
as an ammonia slip. The branching fraction of reactions R 2.4 and R 2.5 is increased by
increasing temperatures. This means that the reaction sequence above becomes chain
branching. Hereby, a signiﬁcant reduction in NO can be obtained, as NH2, the key amine
5
==PAGE:18==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
radical, reacts almost solely with NO. If the temperature is raised further, beyond the
optimum value, the oxidation of NHi radicals to NO is promoted, which results in an
increase of NO. Thus, it is crucial for the performance of SNCR, that it is operated in the
temperature range of eﬀective NO reduction, as the selectivity for N2 or NO is strongly
dependent on the reaction temperature. Even though the eﬃciency is less than optimum,
it is recommended and practiced to operate at a temperature slightly higher than the
optimum. This is practiced in order to minimize the emission byproducts, especially the
NH3 slip [16]. The temperature window of SNCR operation is depicted in Figure 1.
Figure 1: Schematic of NOx reduction as a function of temperature, along with NH3-slip.
Adapted from [15].
2.2.2
Eﬀect of NH3/NO molar ratio
Muzio et al. have been using a laboratory-scale ﬂow reactor to investigate the impact on
NOx reduction of the molar ratio of NH3 injected to initial NO [25]. The ratio is denoted
βNH3 and is deﬁned as βNH3 = [NH3]/[NO]. The results of the study indicate that the
largest reductions of NO occur for βNH3 up to 2. Only slight increases are seen in the
reduction for increments of βNH3 above 2. However, the NH3 slip increases drastically
for increments of βNH3 above 2. The results of NO reduction and NH3 slip is depicted in
Figure 2.
6
==PAGE:19==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 2: Nitric oxide reductions and NH3 slip as a function of βNH3. Initial nitric oxide
level of 500 ppm and excess oxygen of 2%. Left: NH3 slip. Right: nitric oxide reduction
[25].
For βNH3 greater than one, the excess ammonia was found to eﬀectively pass unreacted
through the test section in the temperature region between 1145 K and 1240 K. From the
ammonia slip in Figure 2 (left), it is seen that the decreased eﬀectiveness of ammonia in
NO reduction at temperatures above 1240 K is caused by a competition for the injected
ammonia by excess oxygen. It was found that the ammonia slip is eﬀectively equal to the
injected amount of ammonia minus the amount used for NO reduction at 1145 K. However,
the ammonia slip diminishes as the temperature is increased so that at temperatures above
1300 K, the emissions of ammonia is always below 10 ppm.
2.2.3
Eﬀect of residence time
Duo et al. have investigated the impact of residence time on the performance of SNCR of
NO [26]. It was found, that by prolonging the residence time, the optimum temperatures
are decreased, whereas the maximum reduction of NO is increased. Similar results have
been found by Liang et al., who have been using a laboratory-scale laminar ﬂow reactor
to study the eﬀect on SNCR performance of varying the residence time [27]. The results
from the investigation is found in Figure 3.
7
==PAGE:20==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 3: NO concentration as a function of temperature for various residence times with
initial conditions of: NO=400 ppm, NH3=480 ppm, O2=3 vol%, carrier N2. The residence
time is calculated for each data series by inserting T in Kelvin. Solid lines drawn to guide
data trends [27].
From Figure 3 it is seen that the optimum temperature is initially shifted towards lower
values for prolonged residence time.
However, an asymptotic value for the optimum
temperature is reached for suﬃciently long residence time. Furthermore, the reduction
of NO is increased by a prolonged residence time, although the impact on the reduction
becomes indistinct if longer residence time is available.
2.2.4
Eﬀect of oxygen
As well as the eﬀect of residence time, Liang et al. investigated the impact of varying the
oxygen concentration on the performance of the SNCR process [27]. In the experiments,
the initial concentration of O2 varies from 1% to 6% with an increment of 1% between
experiment run series. The experimental data is plotted in Figure 4. From the results it
is seen that for all initial O2 concentrations, the NO reduction begins at approximately
1048 K and the minimum NO concentration is found at a temperature of approximately
1173 K. At increasing temperatures, the NO concentration increases in a similar fashion
for all initial oxygen levels. Furthermore, it is seen that the NO reduction is promoted in
an atmosphere which is oxygen rich at temperatures below 1148 K. Below this tempera-
ture, the reduction is sensitive to changes in the initial oxygen concentration, especially
around 1098 K. Above 1148 K an oxygen rich atmosphere seems to suppress reduction
of NO. However, less sensitivity of NO reduction towards initial oxygen concentration is
seen above 1148 K relative to below 1148 K. The maximum reduction, at the optimum
temperature of 1173 K for these experiments, is larger when less initial oxygen is present.
8
==PAGE:21==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 4: NO concentration as a function of temperature for various initial concentrations
of oxygen. Initial conditions: NO=400 ppm, NH3=480 ppm, carrier N2. Residence time
of τ (s) = 1024/T with T inserted in Kelvin. Solid lines drawn to guide date trends [27].
Lu and Lu have made similar investigations of the eﬀect of oxygen concentration on the
SNCR process [28]. Their investigations used a temperature range of 1073 −1473 K, a
residence time of 0.323 −0.426 s and, compared to Liang et al. [27], a wider range of
initial oxygen concentration of 0.01 −9.3%. The results of the study are found in Figure
5.
9
==PAGE:22==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 5: Final NO concentration divided by the initial oxygen concentration as a function
of temperature for various initial concentrations of oxygen. Initial conditions: NO=200
ppm, NH3=240 ppm, remainder Ar. Residence time of 0.323 −0.426 s [28].
The wider range of initial oxygen concentrations shows that even trace amounts of 0.01%
O2 is suﬃcient to achieve a 95% reduction of NO. However, the NO reduction using
only 0.01% of initial oxygen took place at signiﬁcantly higher temperatures, with an
optimum at approximately 1373 K, compared to an optimum temperature of around
1223 K using 0.30% of initial oxygen. Thus, as the oxygen levels increase, the temperature
windows are widened and shifted to lower temperatures. Otherwise, the trends conform
to those found by Liang et al.
[27] with signiﬁcant NO reduction sensitivity towards
oxygen concentration below a certain temperature (at conditions for Lu and Lu about
1123 K). Below approximately 1123 K, NO reduction is promoted in O2 rich atmospheres.
Furthermore, also conforming to the trend found by Liang et al. [27], the NO reduction
is suppressed by increasing oxygen concentration, but with less sensitivity.
2.2.5
Eﬀect of CO
Both Alzueta et al. [24] and Caton et al. [29] have investigated the inﬂuence of the CO
concentration on the NO reduction by ammonia.
From the studies, it has been found that the temperature for maximum NO reduction
decreases with higher concentrations of CO [29]. For temperatures above the optimum,
the NO reduction is reduced with increasing concentrations of CO. Similar results were
reported by Caton and Siebers [30].
The results of the laboratory study of Alzueta et al. [24] are shown in Figure 6.
10
==PAGE:23==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 6: NO concentration as a function of temperature for various initial concentrations
of CO.
Experimental data denoted by symbols, model predictions denoted by lines.
Initial conditions: NO=300 ppm, NH3=300 ppm, O2=4.0%, H2O=4.5%, remainder N2.
Residence time at 1200 K is about 150 ms (molar rate is constant). Pressure of 1.05 atm
[24].
As seen from Figure 6, the temperature of maximum NO reduction is shifted towards
lower temperatures for increasing CO concentrations [24]. However, as the concentration
of CO is increased, the temperature window for NO reduction is narrowed. The concen-
tration of NO at maximum reduction is not signiﬁcantly aﬀected by a change in the CO
concentration.
The cause of the decrease in optimum temperature for NO reduction can be found in
the strongly chain branching reaction sequence for moist oxidation of CO, described by
[24, 31]:
CO + OH⇌CO2 + H
(R 2.8)
H + O2⇌O + OH
(R 2.9)
O + H2O⇌OH + OH
(R 2.10)
yielding the net reaction:
CO + O2 + H2O⇌CO2 + 2 OH
(R 2.11)
Due to the enhanced production of OH radicals, which can be used in reaction R 2.3, the
presence of CO promotes the SNCR chemistry at lower temperatures and thereby shifts
the NO reduction regime to lower temperatures [24, 31].
11
==PAGE:24==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
2.2.6
Eﬀect of other combustible additives
Duo et al. have investigated the impact of the addition of combustible additives to an
SNCR system [32]. The eﬀect of diﬀerent alkanes, alcohols and other miscellaneous ad-
ditives has been studied. It applies for all of the studied combustible additives, that a
downward shift of the temperature window is observed as well as a decrease in the NO
reduction eﬃciency. A plot illustrating the eﬀect of using alkanes and alcohols can be
seen in Figure 7. From the left plot of Figure 7, it is seen that the NO reduction both de-
pendents on the type of additive and the concentration of the additives, as approximately
the same concentration of CH4 and C2H6 results in diﬀerent NO reduction performances.
Furthermore, two diﬀerent concentrations of the same additive, C2H6, result in diﬀerent
reduction behaviour, as an increase in the additive concentration shifts the temperature
window further towards lower temperatures. Using diﬀerent alkane additive species with
constant carbon content, the experimental data shows, that CH4 aﬀects the optimum
temperature for NO reduction the least, while C2H6 has the greatest eﬀect. Qualitatively,
Lodder and Lefers draw similar conclusions [33].
OH radicals have been considered important for the propagation of chains in the reduction
of nitric oxides, and thus the overall reaction will be promoted by increasing the OH
radical concentration [32]. Therefore, the study included alcohols to investigate if the
alcohol OH group would aﬀect the performance of the reduction. An additional eﬀect due
to the alcohol OH group was however not observed, when comparing the performance of
NO reduction using alkanes and alcohols. At the optimum temperatures the eﬀects of
C2H4 and (CH2OH)2 was approximately the same. The same observation applies when
comparing CH3CH2OH and C2H6 [32].
Figure 7: NO concentration as a function of temperature using varying alkanes (left)
and alcohols (right). Initial conditions (left): NO=507 ppm, NH3=832 ppm, O2=4% or
(right) NO=507 ppm, NH3=830 ppm, O2=4.2%, H2O≈0.2%. Residence time of 93.3/T s
with T in K [32].
12
==PAGE:25==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
From the results, Duo et al. concluded that in oxygen-rich gasses, combustible additives
will be ignited at lower temperatures than NH3 and more radicals will be produced in
the process. The higher amount of radicals promotes the oxidation of NH3 to NH2, thus
shifting the temperature window towards lower temperatures [32].
2.2.7
Eﬀect of SO2
Dagaut and Nicolle have studied the eﬀect of initial SO2 concentration on the performance
of NO reduction with ammonia in a laboratory-scale jet-stirred reactor [5]. An extract
of the study results are plotted in Figure 8. From the ﬁgure it is seen, that the study
indicates no change in the maximum reduction of NO in the presence of SO2. However,
the optimum temperature is shifted towards higher temperatures. The curves, both ex-
perimental and simulated, are shifted approximately 30 K towards higher temperatures,
by the introduction of 1000 ppm of SO2. The results conform to simulations performed
by Silver [34]. The eﬀect was explained by a model suggesting that SO2 reacts mostly
through the sub-set of reactions of:
SO2 + O (+M) −−→SO3 (+M)
(R 2.12)
H + SO2 + M −−→HOSO + M
(R 2.13)
SO2 + NH2 −−→NH2SO2
(R 2.14)
and the HOSO formed by reaction R 2.13 recycles SO2 by:
HOSO + O2 −−→HO2 + SO2
(R 2.15)
Reactions R 2.13 and R 2.15 combined is equivalent to H+O2+M⇌HO2+M, which reduces
the radical pool and thus the oxidation of NH3. Dagaut and Nicolle suggest that this
mechanism causes the shift toward higher temperatures when SO2 is introduced [5].
13
==PAGE:26==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
Figure 8: NO concentration as a function of temperature with and without SO2. Experi-
mental data denoted by symbols, model predictions denoted by lines. Initial conditions:
NO=500 ppm, NH3=500 ppm, O2=6250 ppm, SO2=0 ppm (open symbols and dotted
line) or SO2=1000 ppm (closed symbols and solid line). Residence time of 100 ms [5].
Results obtained by Li et al. during an experimental and modelling study on the SCNR
performance show a similar shift in the temperature towards higher temperature in the
presence of SO2 [35]. However, the experimental results of the study showed an inhibiting
eﬀect of SO2 on NO reduction, which is conﬂicting to the results of Dagaut and Nicolle
[5] and Silver [34], as the maximum NO reduction is signiﬁcantly decreased in the study
of Li et al. [35].
2.2.8
Eﬀect of mixing
Comparison of the NO reduction eﬃciency of the SNCR process for laboratory-scale
studies and full-scale applications, indicates that mixing between ﬂue gas and reducing
agent is an important factor for eﬃcient reduction of NO. In laboratory-scale studies,
where eﬃcient mixing is achieved, NO reductions above 90% have been achieved [26, 28,
29, 35]. On the other hand, in full-scale SNCR applications, where equal distribution
of reducing agent across the full reactor volume and eﬃcient mixing can be diﬃcult to
achieve, NO reductions of about 30-75% are more common [16].
The inﬂuence of mixing has been investigated by Østberg et al. in a bench scale reac-
tor [36]. The mixing can be separated into macromixing and micromixing. When the
jet is uniformly distributed across the entire reactor cross-section, there is a uniform
concentration at a macroscopic level, and the macromixing is considered perfect. At per-
fect macroscopic mixing, the micromixing is not necessarely perfect, as the injected gas
may be in lumps, and thus the concentrations are not uniform on a molecular level. In
this case micromixing is not perfect, as perfect micromixing is obtained only when the
14
==PAGE:27==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
concentrations on the molecular level are equal to the average concentrations across the
cross-section of the reactor for all species.
In the experiments, the gas containing the reducing ammonia agent was introduced to
the jet in a cross-ﬂow. The momentum ratio is used as a measure of the penetration of
the jet and is given by [36]:
J = ρjU 2
j /ρoU 2
o
(1)
where ρ is the density and U is the linear velocity, while subscript j refer to the jet ﬂow
and subscript o refer to the cross-ﬂow.
From the results of the study by Østberg et al. [36], it was found that increasing the
momentum ratio improves the micromixing and thereby NO reduction up to a limiting
value of J = 20 −30, above which the micromixing is no further improved [36].
The eﬀect of mixing between droplets containing reducing agent and ﬂue gas has also been
investigated in a CFD simulation study of full-scale SNCR system by Lv et al. [37]. In the
study, it has been investigated how the droplet momentum eﬀects the SNCR performance.
The droplet momentum has been adjusted by either changing the total mass ﬂow rate
or the droplet size. The eﬀect of altering the total mass ﬂow rate and droplet size is
shown in Figure 9. It is seen that the NO reduction is higher and the NH3 slip is lower
for increasing total mass ﬂow rate. Furthermore, the NO reduction is increasing and the
NH3 slip is decreasing for an increase in the droplet size up to a certain value, after which
the curve of NO reduction goes down. The droplets are more homogeneously mixed with
ﬂue gas at global scale, as the area covered by droplets is enlarged, by increasing the
droplet size. Thereby the macromixing is improved. However, micromixing between the
droplets and ﬂue gas is a an important factor for SNCR performance as well. The eﬀective
micromixing between reagent droplets and ﬂue gas can be quantiﬁed by:
S =
N

i=1 SP
i
V
(2)
Here V is the volume, SP
i is the surface area of the i’th particle (cm2/m3) and N is the
number of particles within the evaluated volume.
At ﬁrst, the eﬀective mixing is greatly enhanced by increasing the droplet size, because
of the improvement of the droplet local temperature and amounts of droplets joining
in SNCR and then begins a rapid decrease [37].
This follows the well-known mixing
principle. A larger S indicates that the local reagent source is larger and the mixing
between volatilized reagent and local ﬂue gas is faster. Increasing the droplet diameter
from 50 μm to 158 μm leads to better global performance, due to enhanced eﬀective
mixing between reagents and ﬂue gas, combined with better droplet spatial distribution.
However, a further increase to a droplet diameter of 250 μm results in signiﬁcantly small
eﬀective mixing, and the reaction between volatilizing reagents and NO is not adequately
quick.
For increasing total mass ﬂow rate, the number of reagent droplets increases, resulting in
improved eﬀective mixing between the reducing agent and NO and thereby improved NO
15
==PAGE:28==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
reduction.
Figure 9: NO reduction and NH3 slip as a function of (a) total mass ﬂow and (b) droplet
diameter [37].
Lv et al. also found, that the mixing and overall performance of SNCR can be improved
by optimizing the nozzle arrangement injecting the reducing agent [37]. By injecting the
reducing agent via a multi-level nozzle arrangement, a larger area of the chamber can be
covered. Thus, the global mixing is enhanced and as a result the NO reduction increases,
while the ammonia slip decreases.
2.2.9
Mechanisms and modelling of SNCR with ammonia
Simple global kinetics
The homogeneous reaction between NO and NH3 in the presence of excess oxygen in
an isothermal plug ﬂow reactor has been studied by Duo et al. and using the studies,
the kinetics of the reaction has been modelled [26]. The kinetic model has been set up
according to the overall reactions of the SCNR process, that can be described by two
reactions, which are competitive for NH3 and successive for NO [26]:
NH3 + NO + 1
4 O2 −−→N2 + 3
2 H2O
(R 2.16)
NH3 + 5
4 O2 −−→NO + 3
2 H2O
(R 2.17)
A power law model has been used to describe the reaction kinetics of reactions R 2.16 and
R 2.17 [26]. In the temperature range concerned, it has been assumed that the reverse
of the reactions is negligible. Furthermore, a constant concentration of oxygen must be
in great excess during the process. An approximation of ﬁrst order oxidation of NH3 to
NO and second order reduction of NO to N2 has been used, resulting in a good data ﬁt.
Thus, the rates of the gas phase reactions can be described by [26]:
rNO = kox [NH3] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(3)
rNH3 = −kox [NH3] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(4)
16
==PAGE:29==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
In Equations (3) and (4), kr and kox may be dependent on temperature, oxygen concen-
tration and total pressure [26]. In an isothermal plug ﬂow system, the concentration of
NO and NH3 can be described by a system of ordinary diﬀerential equations given by[26]:
rNO = d[NO]
dt
= kox [NH3] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(5)
rNH3 = d[NH3]
dt
= −kox [NH3] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(6)
Estimation of the parameters kox and kr have been made using the least-square method
based on experimental data. In the study of Duo et al. [26], the values of kox and kr
were estimated at 5 diﬀerent temperatures between 1140 and 1335 K. The pressure was
atmospheric and the oxygen concentration was approximately 4% by volume. It has been
found that the Arrhenius law may be used to describe the temperature dependencies of
the reaction rate coeﬃcients, as plotting of ln(kox) and ln(kr) as a function of 1/T has
yielded an approximately straight line. Thus, the reaction rate constants can be described
by [26]:
kox = A1 · exp

(7)

kox = (2.21 ± 0.33) · 1014 · exp

The model was compared to other experimental results, than the results used to estimate
the parameters [26]. This was done in order to evaluate the kinetic model. Some of the
results of the comparison are depicted in Figure 10. It is seen that the model predicts
slightly higher NO reduction below a temperature of approximately 1150 K, while the
predicted NO reduction is slightly lower than the experimental at temperatures between
1150 and 1300 K. However, overall the model predictions are good.

The model has produced satisfactory prediction of NO reductions and reasonably good
prediction of NH3 conversions, when comparing to pilot plant experiments [26]. However,
the NH3 breakthrough was overestimated, which may be caused by diﬀerent conditions
in the pilot plant relative to the laboratory experiments, as H2O vapor, combustion ash,
unburnt fragments of fuel, CO, CO2, etc. is most likely present in the pilot reactor ﬂue
gas. Furthermore, the assumption of plug ﬂow may not be adequate for the pilot reactor
and the radial temperature proﬁle is not considered in the model.
Another study by Brouwer et al. has developed a reduced chemical kinetic model for
the prediction of SNCR chemistry [38]. The ammonia pathway in the reduced SNCR
mechanism is given by:
NH3 + NO −−→N2 + H2O + H
(R 2.18)
NH3 + O2 −−→NO + H2O + H
(R 2.19)
The NO reducing reaction R 2.18 is dominating at lower temperatures, while reaction
R 2.19, which produces NO, dominates at higher temperatures [38]. For these reactions,
the rate parameters were determined by a detailed sensitivity analysis performed with a
full chemical kinetic model for conditions in the typical SNCR operation range. The full
chemical kinetic model used to derive the reduced kinetic model, consist of the kinetic
rates of Miller and Bowman [18] with modiﬁcations from the literature [39, 40], which
were applied to SENKIN [41] to model the complete SNCR chemistry under quenched or
isothermal conditions.
In the model, the formation of NO and NH3 is given by [38]:
rNO = d[NO]
dt
= kox [NH3] [O2] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(9)
rNH3 = d[NH3]
dt
= −kox [NH3] [O2] −kr [NH3] [NO]
(10)

Eﬀect of additives [26]
As described in section 2.2.6 the addition of combustible additives shifts the NO reduction
temperature downwards [32]. This eﬀect has also been modelled and simulated by Duo
et al. [26]. The downwards shift in NO reduction temperature, which is to the same
extent as the downwards shift in NH3 conversion temperature, indicates that ignition of
the reaction mixture occurs at lower temperatures in the presence of additives [26, 32].

addition of combustible additives. Thus, the reaction rate constants of Equations (7) and

Detailed chemistry for SNCR
The modelling of the simple global kinetics does not take into account all the individual
elementary reactions undergoing in the reduction of NO. Therefore, it is important to also
look at the more detailed mechanisms of the SNCR chemistry. A lot of diﬀerent chemical
kinetic schemes have been proposed in the literature for modelling the complexity of
SNCR chemistry. An example is Miller and Bowman [18], which suggested a mechanism
with 103 species and 234 reactions. This work included the thermal, prompt and fuel
NOx formation, as well as the thermal part of the NOx reduction. In this mechanism the
importance of the NNH radical in the NO chemical paths played an important role. By
quantiﬁcation of the characteristic times representative of the NNH radical and clarifying
the understanding of the interactions between NH2 and NO, the model was improved by
Miller and Glarborg [20, 43]. Additional studies on the characteristic time of NNH have
been conducted by Klippenstein et al. [19]. From the results, a reliable kinetic scheme of
the interaction between ammonia related component and nitrogen oxide molecules have
been proposed, involving 34 species and 207 reactions.
Recently Glarborg et al. published a kinetic model [44], largely adopted from Klippenstein
et al. [19]. The main diﬀerence from this model to the earlier kinetic models of Miller
and Bowman [18], and Miller and Glarborg [43], is that, rather than being restricted by
reactions of NNH with O2 or NO, the chain branching in the process is now restricted
mainly by the small value of the branching fraction α of the NH2 +NO reaction. Further-
more, an NNH lifetime of 10−9 s is used following Klippenstein et al. [19]. This is much
lower than values commonly used in modelling [44]. A pathway diagram for the Thermal
21
==PAGE:34==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
DeNOx process in the presence of water vapor is depicted in Figure 13.
Figure 13: Pathway diagram illustrating the reactions for the Thermal DeNOx process.
Pathways only important at high temperatures are denoted by dashed lines [44].
NH2 is formed as ammonia reacts with the O/H radical pool, primarily OH via:
NH3 + OH ⇌NH2 + H2O
(R 2.20)
The key step in the process is the subsequent reaction between NH2 and NO, which must
remove NO and produce free radicals simultaneously to sustain reaction [18, 44]. The
reaction has two product channels:
NH2 + NO ⇌NNH + OH
(R 2.21)
NH2 + NO ⇌N2 + H2O
(R 2.22)
Reaction R 2.21 ads to a net formation of three hydroxyl redicals, as dissociation of NNH
initiates a reaction sequence of [18, 44]:
NNH ⇌N2 + H
(R 2.23)
H + O2 ⇌O + OH
(R 2.24)
O + H2O ⇌OH + OH
(R 2.25)
It is required that the branching fraction of the NH2 + NO reaction, α = kR 2.21/(kR 2.21 +
kR 2.22), must be at least 25%, in order for the overall process to be self-sustainable [44].
22
==PAGE:35==
2.2
Selective non-catalytic reduction
In the temperature range of 1100 −1400 K, it is now well established that the value of α
is 0.3 −0.4.
A strong growth in the O/H radical pool is experienced in the high temperature end, as
the chain branching cycle occurs too rapidly, hereby promoting the conversion of NH2 to
NH [44]:
NH2 + OH ⇌NH + H2O
(R 2.26)
This reaction competes with the NH2 + NO reaction. As seen in Figure 13, the formed
NH is partly oxidized to NO. This follows the sequence of [19]:
NH + O2 ⇌NO + OH
(R 2.27)
NH + O2 ⇌HNO + O
(R 2.28)
HNO + M ⇌H + NO + M
(R 2.29)
HNO + OH ⇌NO + H2O
(R 2.30)
The following reactions are another source of NO formation [44]:
NH2 + O ⇌HNO + H
(R 2.31)
NH2 + O ⇌NH + OH
(R 2.32)
The products are partly oxidized to NO via the sequence of reactions R 2.27-R 2.30 at
temperatures around 1400 K, resulting in a net formation of NO.
Klippenstein et al. furthermore presented the NNH mechanism for forming NO [19]:
NNH + O ⇌NH + NO
(R 2.33)
Table 2 summarizes the reactions reviewed in this section.
This section does not cover the complete set of elementary reactions undergoing in the
SNCR process. However, it covers some of the most important reactions.
23
==PAGE:36==

2.3
Thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate
Halstead have studied the thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate [46]. Ammonium
sulfate was dried at 383 K and placed in a platinum boat inside a quartz tube, which
was wrapped with Kanthal heating strip and insulated with asbestos. The apparatus was
rapidly heated to 673 K, at which temperature all of the decomposition experiments were
carried out. The time for heating was normally less than 10% of the total experiment
time. The gaseous products were ﬂushed from the tube into two Drechsel bottles, in
which the products were dissolved in diﬀerent reagents. Dilute hydrochloric acid was
used to detect ammonia, acidiﬁed iodine was used to detect reducing agents, such as
SO2, while an acidiﬁed barium chloride solution was used to detect SO3, as this would
precipitate as barium sulfate.
Residues, which did not evaporate, were analyzed for
ammonia and sulfate contents, acidity by titrating with sodium hydroxide and reducing
properties by using potassium permanganate. Furthermore, a number of their infrared
spectra were determined. The results of the study indicated, that the formation of SO3
during decomposition is not signiﬁcant. Furthermore, the study indicated that the thermal
decomposition and vaporization of ammonium sulfate takes place according to two distinct
sets of reactions.
Initially, ammonium sulfate is converted to ammonium pyrosulfate,
ammonia and water[46]:
2 (NH4)2SO4 −−→(NH4)2S2O7 + 2 NH3 + H2O
(R 2.34)
This is followed by the decomposition of ammonium pyrosulfate, which was suggested to
take place according to[46]:
3 (NH4)2S2O7 −−→2 NH3 + 6 SO2 + 2 N2 + 9 H2O
(R 2.35)
24
==PAGE:37==
2.3
Thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate
Thus, the total decomposition of ammonium sulfate may be expressed by [46]:
3 (NH4)2SO4 −−→4 NH3 + 3 SO2 + 6 H2O + N2
(R 2.36)
More recently, Wu et al.
have studied the decomposition of ammonium sulfate [47].
The thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate in an N2 atmosphere was studied in
a thermogravimetric analyzer (TGA) using a fast heating rate. Ammonium sulfate was
heated rapidly to a predeﬁned temperature and subsequently kept isothermally. The end
temperature was varied between 473 and 623 K, with a heating rate varying between
50-200 K/min.
The yields of SO2 and SO3 from the ammonium sulfate thermal decomposition were
studied in a laboratory-scale tube reactor [47]. A fractional conversion of sulfur of the
sulfate to SO2 under the given temperature was calculated on the basis of the analyzed
SO2 concentration over time. It was assumed, that the remaining sulfur in sulfate was
released as SO3.
A model has been set up, where it has been assumed that the decomposition of ammonium
sulfate is kinetically controlled under boiler conditions and follow the volumetric reaction
model of [47]:
dW

(14)
Here, W is the relative mass of anhydrous sulfate, which is deﬁned as:
W = m −m∞

(15)
where m is the mass of dehydrated sulfate at t during decomposition, m∞is the mass
of residue when the sulfate is completely decomposed and m0 is the mass of dehydrated
sulfate before decomposition.
The rate constant in Equation (14) can be described by the Arrhenius equation [47]:

of kJ/mol, the gas constant, R, in units of kJ/(mol K) and the particle temperature, T,
in units of K.
Using the results of fast heating TGA experiments, the activation energy and pre-exponential
factor for ammonium sulfate decomposition was determined and the resulting rate con-

Kiyoura and Urano have suggested that the decomposition products of ammonium sul-
fate consist of NH3, SO3 and H2O along with other gaseous species, based on diﬀerential
thermal analysis (DTA) curves and thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curves [48]. Gas
chromatography and chemical analysis was used to qualitatively analyze the released
gases. The amounts of H– and SO 2–
4

used to analyze the amounts of NH +
4
in solutions of the heated residues.
The stud-
ies concluded, that initially deammoniation occurs producing ammonium bisulfate. The
ammonium bisulfate was then decomposed into ammonium pyrosulfate by dehydration.
Finally, ammonium pyrosulfate was decomposed further into gases.
2.4
Ammonium sulfate as a reducing agent
Ammonium sulfate has previously been investigated for its NOx reducing properties in
SNCR [4] and for its corrosion reducing properties [49]. Furthermore, a study by Kassman
et al. have considered the simultaneous reduction of NOx and the corrosive species KCl,
using ammonium sulfate as a reducing agent [50].
Vattenfall has developed and patented a concept with a sulfate containing compound
called “ChlorOut” [49]. The compound is sprayed into the ﬂue gas in between completion
of combustion and the boiler superheaters, where it reacts and converts KCl into K2SO4.
This reduces corrosion, as K2SO4 is much less corrosive than KCl, due to higher ﬁrst
26
==PAGE:39==
2.4
Ammonium sulfate as a reducing agent
melting point of deposits containing only alkali sulfates relative to deposits containing
alkali chlorides. Henderson et al. have studied the reduction of corrosion in wood-ﬁred
boilers, using ammonium sulfate in the ChlorOut process [49]. The overall reactions of
the study have been described as:
(NH4)2SO4 −−→2 NH3 + SO3 + H2O
(R 2.38)
SO3 + H2O + 2 KCl −−→2 HCl + K2SO4
(R 2.39)
The performance of corrosion reduction using ammonium sulfate was studied by perform-
ing GDOES proﬁling through the oxide layer of 13CrMo44 rings, which had been exposed
to the ﬂue gas in a wood-ﬁred Circulating Fluidized Bed boiler for 3 hours, both with
and without the addition of ammonium sulfate. It was found that the corroded layer was
approximately 2 μm thick without ammonium sulfate compared to 0.5 μm with ammo-
nium sulfate. The composition proﬁles of the rings are shown in Figure 15 and they show
that much less Cl and O is present in the rings, where ammonium sulfate has been used
for treatment of the ﬂue gas. Analytical scanning electron microscopy of the surface has
been used to conﬁrm, that a large number of iron chloride particles were present after
exposure without ammonium sulfate, while these iron chloride particles were absent to a
large extent with ammonium sulfate.
The long-term corrosion of superheater steels in a Bubbling Fluidized Bed boiler in
Nyköping has also been tested [49].
The results are shown in Figure 16.
It is seen,
that on average, the corrosion rate has been approximately halved by using ammonium
sulfate.
The deposit and ﬂue gas chemistry has also been studied in a Circulating Fluidized Bed
(CFB) co-ﬁred with bark and waste in Munksund [49]. The experiments showed that
addition of ammonium sulfate reduced the KCl concentration in the ﬂue gas from 20 to
2 ppm, at an injection temperature of 953 K. A large decrease of chlorine content in the
deposits was observed along with a reduction of at least 50% of the deposit growth rate.
In a Bubbling Fluidized Bed boiler in Nyköping with a fuel consisting of 5% coal, 47.5%
demolition wood and 47.5% logging residues, the average gaseous alkali chloride levels in
the ﬂue gas was reduced from 30 to 17 ppm with ammonium sulfate [49]. The Cl content
of the deposit was removed or greatly reduced by converting KCl to K2SO4.

==PAGE:41==
2.4
Ammonium sulfate as a reducing agent
Kassman et al. has studied the simultaneous reduction of KCl and NOx during biomass
combustion in a 12 MW Circulating Fluidized Bed boiler [50]. The NO emission from co-
ﬁring of wood pellets, wood chips and straw using (NH4)2SO4, NH3 and urea as reducing
agents is depicted in Figure 17a. Using (NH4)2SO4, NO was reduced to less than half at a
molar ratio of NH3/NO of approximately 3, while KCl simultaneously was reduced from 45
to 15 ppm at a temperature of 1123 K. Sulfur granules were added in parallel during testing
with urea and ammonia, in order to achieve similar conditions to that of (NH4)2SO4 with
respect to KCl reduction. At a NH3/NO molar ratio of approximately 2, urea performed
better than ammonia and ammonium sulfate with respect to NO reduction. Ammonium
sulfate performed better than sulfur with respect to KCl reduction, as KCl was reduced
from 45 to 15 ppm at a S/Cl molar ratio of 1.8 with ammonium sulfate compared to a
reduction from 45 to 20 ppm at a S/Cl molar ratio of 3.8 with sulfur. Thus, even at
less than half the S/Cl molar ratio, sulfation of KCl was more eﬃcient with ammonium
sulfate compared to sulfur. It was suggested, that ammonium sulfate performed better
than sulfur, because it decomposed into SO3.
The NO emission from co-ﬁring of wood pellets and wood chips using (NH4)2SO4, NH3 and
urea as reducing agents is depicted in Figure 17b. Without straw, the chlorine content was
not high enough for the formation of KCl. Thus, in this case PVC was used to generate
high levels of chlorine, which was necessary for formation of KCl. When using ammonium
sulfate in this case, the KCl level decreased, while it remained unchanged using urea and
ammonia. It is seen from the ﬁgure, that the reduction capacity of the three additives
were very similar in this case.
A concluding remark of the study was that ammonium sulfate made it possible to reduce
both the emission of NO, as seen in Figure 17, and the concentration of KCl.
The
decreased concentration of KCl will result in less corrosion.
Figure 17: NO emissions as a function of molar ratio of NH3 to NO with injection of
diﬀerent reducing agents. Temperature of 1123 K, excess air ratio of 1.2. Fuel in a)
mixture of wood pellets and wood chips and straw, b) mixture of wood pellets and wood
chips. In a) S-granules were added during urea and NH3, in b) PVC was added [50].

==PAGE:42==
2.5
Summary of literature review
2.5
Summary of literature review
During combustion of waste, high amounts of NOx and especially fuel NO is formed. Strict
regulations on NOx emissions have been introduced, in order to reduce the emissions of
NOx.
SNCR is the most commonly used method for reduction of NOx in waste incineration
plants. The most common reducing agents in the SNCR process are ammonia and urea.
The signiﬁcant inﬂuencing factors of the SNCR process are highlighted below:
• Generally, high SNCR performance is achieved by operating within a temperature
window of approximately 1100 −1400 K.
• The molar ratio of NH3 to NO is of importance for the performance of the SNCR
process. From laboratory-scale studies in the literature, it has been found that the
best performance is achieved for molar ratios up to 2.
• Studies have found, that the optimum temperature for reduction of NOx decrease,
while the maximum reduction is increased, by prolonging the residence time avail-
able for the SNCR process.
• Increasing the initial oxygen concentration of the SNCR system have been found
to widen the temperature window for eﬀective reduction and shift it towards lower
temperatures.
However, the maximum NO reduction is decreased by increasing
oxygen concentration.
• Generally, the literature have reported that the temperature window for SNCR
is shifted towards higher temperatures by the introduction of SO2. Most of the
reviewed studies have not found a change in the maximum NO reduction by the
presence of SO2. However, an inhibiting eﬀect of SO2 on NO reduction has been
reported in one of the reviewed studies.
• Combustible additives have been found to shift the temperature window of SNCR
towards lower temperatures and decrease the NO reduction eﬃciency.
• Increasing the total mass ﬂow rate improves the eﬀective mixing between reducing
agent and NO, which leads to improved reduction. Increasing the droplet size to a
certain value leads to enhanced eﬀective mixing and thus better global performance
of the SNCR process. However, when the droplets become too large, the eﬀective
mixing becomes too small and the eﬃciency of the NO reduction decreases.
Modelling of the SNCR kinetics have been found in the literature and a couple of mod-
els have been reviewed. Some models covering the simple global kinetics of the SNCR
process have been reviewed. Also the eﬀect of combustible additives have been modelled.
Furthermore, the detailed chemistry of SNCR has been reviewed, where the NH2 + NO
reaction and the NNH radical are of great importance.
30
==PAGE:43==
2.5
Summary of literature review
The thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate has also been reviewed. Some discrep-
ancy has been found in the literature. It is generally accepted, that ammonia and water
vapor is produced from thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate. However, it is not
clear from the review of the literature if the remaining products are mainly SO2, SO3 or
a mixture of these species depending on the decomposition conditions.
Finally, from the literature review, it has been found that ammonium sulfate can be used
for the simultaneous reduction of NOx and corrosion.
By converting KCl to the much less corrosive K2SO4, it has been found that ammonium
sulfate can decrease the corrosion to a large extent.
Furthermore, it has been found that ammonium sulfate can eﬃciently be used as a re-
ducing agent of NOx in the SNCR process.
31
==PAGE:44==
3
Experimental setup and procedures
In this section, a detailed description of the experimental setup and procedures used to
perform experiments is presented.
Based on the ﬁndings of the literature review, it has been decided to experimentally in-
vestigate the SNCR process using ammonium sulfate as reducing agent, as the availability
of experimental data on this subject, in the literature, is limited. Thus, it has been of
interest to develop a laboratory-scale setup, which can feed micron-sized droplets of am-
monium sulfate in an aqueous solution into a heated reactor, in order to run continuous
SNCR experiments.
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
In order to experimentally investigate the performance of an SNCR system with ammo-
nium sulfate as reducing agent, it was necessary to develop a feeding system capable of
feeding micron-sized droplets of aqueous ammonium sulfate solution to a heated reactor.
The practical solution to this problem has been to construct a sealed container for am-
monium sulfate solution, with an ultrasonic humidiﬁer capable of generating micron-sized
droplets. The droplets can then be transferred to the reactor with a carrier gas ﬂow. The
ﬁnal setup is shown in Figure 18. The main features of the setup are:
1. Mist generation system.
2. Homogeneous ﬂow reactor. The reactor is placed inside an oven.
3. Gas analysis system. This cannot be seen in Figure 18, but is shown in the schematic
drawing in Figure 19.
Besides these features, tubing has been used to transport various gases and ammonium
sulfate mist to the reactor, and the outlet gas to an FTIR analyzer. Heat tracing equip-
ment has been used for the outlet gas tubing and an FTIR analyzer has been used to
determine the composition of the outlet gas.
To give an easier overview of the full experimental setup, a schematic drawing of the setup
is shown in Figure 19.
The working principle of the diﬀerent parts of the setup will be described in details in the
following sections, along with the progress in the setup development along the way.

3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
3.1.1
Mist generation system
The mist generation system is a combination of a mist generator and a mist tank. The
working principle of the system is described in this section.
Generation of mist
An important mechanism of the feeding system is the generation of ammonium sulfate
solution mist. The mist has been generated by use of a 24 V ultrasonic humidiﬁer from
Shineus, depicted in Figure 20 [51]. The principle of operation of ultrasonic humidiﬁers
is, that a piezoelectric transducer is immersed in a water bed, where it converts high
frequency, electrical signals into high frequency mechanical oscillations [52]. A momentary
vacuum and strong compression occur, as the oscillation speed increases to a level at
which the water particles cannot follow the oscillating surface, which leads to an explosive
formation of air bubbles (cavitation). At this point, broken capillary waves are generated,
resulting in the formation of a mist, as droplets of approximately 1 micron in diameter
break the surface tension of the liquid and dissipates quickly into the air.
Figure 20: Shineus ultrasonic humidiﬁer [51].
Mist tank
During normal operation, the mist generator does not produce a steady ﬂow of mist.
However, in order to obtain reliable experimental data of the SNCR process, the amount
of reducing ammonium sulfate agent, and thus the mist ﬂow, has to be approximately
constant. Therefore, a mist tank has been used to ensure an approximately steady ﬂow
of ammonium sulfate mist into the reactor. It is simply a closed tank in which the mist
generator has been placed. The tank has been approximately ﬁlled to 1/3 of its volume
with ammonium sulfate solution. The other 2/3 of the volume is available for mist upon
generation. Two tubes are connected to the top of the tank. One is an inlet for N2 carrier
gas. The other is an outlet, where the carrier gas along with the mist is transported to
the reactor.
The overall mist generation system of the mist generator and mist tank combined has
been developed based on a similar system in Lund University for generating a mist of
34
==PAGE:47==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
micron-sized droplets from an aqueous KCl solution [53].
3.1.2
Homogeneous ﬂow reactor
The reactor used for the experiments is a vertical laboratory-scale quartz homogeneous
ﬂow reactor. A schematic drawing of the reactor is shown in Figure 21 and the dimensions
of the reactor can be found in Appendix 7.3. The reactor is mounted with a top piece,
which allows for two inlet feeds. This is an inlet for a mixture of N2 and O2 carrier gas
with ammonium sulfate mist and an inlet for a gas mixture of N2, NO and/or other gases
such as NH3 and SO2, which can be varied in order to achieve the desired initial species
concentration in the reactor. At initial SNCR experiments, the oxygen was sent to the
reactor in a mixture with N2 and NO. However, signiﬁcant amounts of NO2 were formed
through reaction of NO and O2. Thus, to reduce the mixing time of NO and O2, the
O2 ﬂow was combined with the N2 carrier ﬂow, which ﬂows through the mist tank and
this signiﬁcantly reduced the amount of NO2 formed. The inlet gas ﬂows are controlled
by EL-FLOW® Select mass ﬂow meters [54]. The reactor has been placed inside an oven,
which is equipped with three electrical heating elements (one in the top part, one in the
center and one in the bottom part of the oven), in order to enable the adjustment of
the temperature to maintain a uniform temperature proﬁle throughout the reactor. The
outlet gas from the reactor is sent to an FTIR spectroscopy gas analyzer.
35
==PAGE:48==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
Figure 21: Schematic layout of reactor along with top piece and heating elements.
3.1.3
Gas analysis system
The gas analysis system consists of heat traced tubing and an FTIR analyzer. The heat
traced tubing is used to transport the outlet gas from the reactor to the FTIR.
Heat tracing
In order to get reliable information regarding the reducing reaction using ammonium
sulfate, it was important that all of the outlet gas has been transported to the FTIR
analyzer. Furthermore, it has been important that the diﬀerent species of the outlet gas
did not react between the reactor outlet and the FTIR analyzer.
From the literature study, it is clear that NH3 is one of the products of thermal ammonium
sulfate decomposition.
It is known that NH3 can adsorb to surfaces such as Teﬂon,
36
==PAGE:49==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
which is the material of the tubes [55].
Such adsorption would be a source of error
in the experimental data. However, experimental investigation suggests that increasing
temperature results in decreasing adsorption of NH3 [55]. Thus, heat tracing of the reactor
outlet tube has been applied in order to minimize the adsorption of NH3.
Furthermore, condensation of the water vapor from the ammonium sulfate solution mist
in the reactor outlet tube constituted a problem during initial experiments without heat
tracing. As the gas was transported from the reactor to the analyzer, the gas was cooled
by the ambient air, which resulted in condensation of water. It is desired to avoid such
a liquid phase in the tubing, as it may interact with, and enable absorption of, product
gases. By heat tracing the tube above the boiling point of water, the water vapor will not
condense.
Due to the presence of water vapor and SO2 and/or SO3 from ammonium sulfate decom-
position, sulfurous acid and/or sulfuric acid may be formed [56]. These acids condense at
temperatures approximately between 388 and 433 K. Thus, the outlet tube has to be heat
traced to a temperature above 433 K in order to ensure prevention of such condensation.
The temperature set point for the heat tracing was chosen to be 453 K. This is above the
condensation temperature of both the water vapor and the acids considered. However, it
is lower than the FTIR gas cell temperature of 464 K, such that potential condensation
of gases does not occur inside the FTIR gas cell. The heat tracing is performed by sur-
rounding the outlet tube by a heating element. The tubing and heating element has then
been insulated by insulation wool and tin foil. As the tube was approximately 2 m long,
the temperature had small ﬂuctuations along the tube. Therefore, three thermocouples
were placed along the tube, one in the beginning, one in the middle and one in the end
of the tube, to ensure that the temperature was approximately the same along the tube.
The thermocouples were placed in direct contact with both the heating element and the
tube. This was essential, as one of the thermocouples was used to control the tempera-
ture of the heating element. If the thermocouple was not in direct contact with the tube,
the temperature of the heating element would become signiﬁcantly higher than expected,
which could result in melting of the tube. It was found that during steady operation, the
temperature ﬂuctuated approximately by 5 −10 K.
FTIR analyzer
A Fourier Transform Infrared Spectrometer, also known as an FTIR gas analyzer, has
been used to analyze the outlet gas from the reactor. The objective of the experiments
was to investigate the thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate in the temperature
range of interest for SNCR and the SNCR process using ammonium sulfate as reducing
agent. Thus, it has been necessary to quantify the composition of the outlet gas. The
FTIR analyzer used during the experiments is a MultiGas™2030 FTIR Continuous Gas
Analyzer [57]. The eﬀective optical path length of the FTIR gas cell is 5.11 m and the
volume is 200 mL. At the gas cell temperature of operation, which is 191 ◦C, the analyzer
has been calibrated to accurate measuring of the species, which may be of interest during
the experiments. The upper range of the calibrations is listed in Table 3. The FTIR has
37
==PAGE:50==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
been calibrated using span gases and the accuracy is ±2% up to the upper range of the
calibration [57].
Table 3: Concentrations up to which the FTIR has been calibrated for accurate measure-
ments for species, which may be relevant to this project.
Species
Upper range of calibration

3000 ppm
NH3
3000 ppm

300 ppm

150 ppm
H2O
20%
NO2
150 ppm
H2SO4
50 ppm
The FTIR makes a measurement of the gas composition every 0.98 s. In order to reduce
the uncertainty of the results, an average of 4 measurements is logged and used for data
processing during all experiments. This means that a data point has been logged every
3.92 s.
3.1.4
Test of droplet formation
A crucial part of the setup is the transport of ammonium sulfate mist from the mist tank
to the reactor. As described in section 3.1.1, the mist is transported using a carrier gas
stream. The gas along with the mist is then transported to the reactor via tubing. It is
important to have a gas ﬂow, which is suﬃciently high in order to transport a signiﬁcant
amount of mist to the reactor. However, it was found during preliminary experiments,
that a too high gas ﬂow would cause formation of larger droplets on the tubing walls. It
was found that larger droplets were formed at a gas ﬂow of 1 NL/min.
The conﬁguration of the tubing leading the mist to the reactor was found to be another
point of concern considering droplet formation.
The conﬁguration during preliminary
tests is shown in Figure 22a. In this conﬁguration the tubing experienced three bends.
The bends were found to be the main cause for droplet formation. The mist condensed
into larger droplets, which especially gathered around the downwards facing bend. The
merging of small mist droplets into larger droplets is a concern as the ﬂow of ammonium
sulfate to the reactor is not steady when larger droplets are formed. The larger droplets
were not uniform in size and were not fed to the reactor at a constant rate. Further-
more, larger droplets would require a longer residence time for thermal decomposition of
ammonium sulfate, as the evaporation of water would become more time consuming.
During a preliminary experiment with the tubing conﬁguration of Figure 22a it was tested
if the mist ﬂow was constant. The conditions of the N2 carrier ﬂow was 1 NL/min and the
reactor temperature was 1223 K. At this temperature it was expected, that signiﬁcant
amounts of SO2 were produced from the decomposition of ammonium sulfate [46, 47].
During this experiment the FTIR analyzer was not available and instead an SO2 analyzer
38
==PAGE:51==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
was used to analyze the outlet gas. In order to test if the mist ﬂow to the reactor was
steady, the SO2 concentration of the outlet gas was measured. At steady operation, the
SO2 concentration would reach a steady value. The results of the preliminary experiment
are shown in Figure 23. It is seen that the SO2 concentration did not become steady
during the experiment. On the other hand, the ﬂuctuations of the concentration increase
as a function of time. This is caused by merging of the small mist particles into larger
droplets, which subsequently are fed to the reactor. The longer the experiment ran, the
larger droplets were formed in the bends of the tubing. The droplets were fed to the
reactor in small ’waves’ giving cause for large signals at the moments when a droplet
enters the reactor, while the signals were smaller at times when no large droplets entered
the reactor. Looking at the tubing, it was clearly visualized that larger droplets were
formed using this conﬁguration of the tubing.
Instead, the tubing conﬁguration of Figure 22b was used for further experiments. This
conﬁguration has only one, relatively smooth, bend.
This signiﬁcantly decreased the
formation of larger droplets and gave a mist ﬂow, which was almost steady. The formation
of droplets was not completely avoided. However, the size and amount of formed droplets
were much smaller. The droplets formed, using this conﬁguration, were to a large extend
attached to the tubing walls and were not continuously fed to the reactor.
However,
occasionally a small droplet was entrained by the carrier ﬂow and was fed to the reactor
along with the mist. This was easily visualized by a small peak in the SO2 signal.
39

conﬁguration of tubing.
