3.1.5
Test of pressure buildup and leaks
Another signiﬁcant concern experienced during the development of the setup was pressure
buildup inside the system, causing leaks. During the initial tests of the system, no notice-
able pressure buildup or leaks were detected. However, after a few days of preliminary
testing of the setup, it became clear that the pressure was built up in the system during
experiments, as the outlet piece of the mist tank was lifted oﬀby the increased pressure
in the system. Thus, all pieces with the potential of getting removed due to the pressure
buildup was secured, either by switching from glass plugs to rubber plugs or by equipping
clams, preventing the pieces from being removed by the pressure inside the system.
As the pieces were secured, the over-pressure was not released and the pressure buildup
inside the system was increased, resulting in small leakages from the system. The leakages
were located and sealed with a Teﬂon tape.
Sealing of the leakages would lead to an even larger pressure buildup, and therefore it was
decided that the reason for the large over-pressure had to be found. It could potentially
become a safety issue, if the pressure buildup became too large, and thus the problem
had to be ﬁxed.
The FTIR analyzer was equipped with a small ﬁlter in order to prevent particles from
entering the analyzer and potentially foul it. A hypothesis was that small particles, of
unknown origin, had been formed during the ammonium sulfate decomposition. If such
particles had clogged the ﬁlter, it would be very diﬃcult for the gas ﬂow to pass through
the ﬁlter to the analyzer and subsequently to the vent. As a consequence, a pressure
buildup inside the system would occur.
The hypothesis was tested by removing the ﬁlter and cleaning it with demineralized water
41
==PAGE:54==
3.1
Development of laboratory-scale setup
in an ultrasonic bath to remove any water soluble particles. The ﬁlter was then dried and
reinserted before the FTIR analyzer. Due to the small size of the ﬁlter, it would quickly be
re-clogged with particles if the hypothesis was conﬁrmed. Therefore, in order to prevent
it from being re-clogged, a larger heated ﬁlter was installed in the system prior to the
small ﬁlter. This ﬁlter was larger in both pore size and surface area. Thereby, it would
not clog as quickly as the small ﬁlter, and it would be possible to conduct experiments
of longer duration. As a safety measure, a manometer was installed between the reactor
outlet and the large ﬁlter. This enabled the measurement of the pressure in the system.
Thus, if the potential pressure buildup became too high, the inlet gas could be shut oﬀ
manually.
After cleaning and re-installing the small ﬁlter, and installing the manometer and the
larger heated ﬁlter, it was tested if the problem of pressure buildup re-occurred. Initially,
only N2 gas was used to test the system. The pressure quickly stabilized at approximately
1.15 atm with a gas ﬂow of 1 NL/min.
Thus, a small over-pressure was present due
to the resistance of the ﬁlters. However, a continuous pressure buildup did not occur.
The same applied when the reactor, mist generator and heat tracing were turned on
and decomposition of ammonium sulfate occurred.
In this case, the pressure quickly
stabilized at approximately 1.15 atm with a gas ﬂow of 0.5 NL/min. The pressure was
steady through an experiment run of 6 hours duration, after which the system was shut
down. No leaks were present during the experiment. Thus, it was concluded that the
pressure buildup had been caused by a clogged ﬁlter.
An experiment run feeding only mist of demineralized water was used to test if the system
could operate with a steady feed of mist. The test was performed using a reactor temper-
ature of 1173 K and a N2 carrier gas ﬂow rate of 0.5 NL/min. The water concentration
in the outlet gas, which was logged by the FTIR analyzer, is depicted in Figure 24. It is
seen that large ﬂuctuations in the water concentration appeared. Such ﬂuctuations had
not been experienced in previous tests. Thus, either the newly added large ﬁlter or the
mist generator, possibly due to wear, posed a problem.
In order to test if it was the newly added ﬁlter, that caused the problem, this was removed.
Tests were then performed, again using water mist. As only water and nitrogen was fed,
no particles would form and the small ﬁlter would not clog. The water concentration in
the outlet gas was logged and the results are depicted in Figure 25. It is clearly seen, that
the problems with large ﬂuctuations did no longer appear, and an almost steady feeding
could be achieved. Thus, it was decided to continue experiments without use of the large
heated ﬁlter. In the case of clogging of the small ﬁlter, it would have to be cleaned before
continuing experiments.
42
==PAGE:55==
3.2
Gas ﬂow calibration
Figure 24: Water concentration as a function of time with large heated ﬁlter equipped.
Reactor temperature of 1173 K and N2 carrier gas ﬂow of 0.5 NL/min.
Figure 25: Water concentration as a function of time without large heated ﬁlter equipped.
Reactor temperature of 1173 K and N2 carrier gas ﬂow of 0.5 NL/min.
3.2
Gas ﬂow calibration
The concentration of diﬀerent gas species in the total gas ﬂow depends on the volumetric
ﬂow of each gas species sent to the reactor. Therefore, it is important to know the inlet
ﬂow of the diﬀerent gases.
In order to obtain accurate inlet ﬂow measurements, the
ﬂow meters, which is used to control the inlet gas ﬂows, have been calibrated using a
Gilibrator-2 system from Sensidyne [58]. The working principle of the calibration system
is, that the gas is sent through a wet cell. This generates a bubble ﬁlm, which moves
through the cell. The ﬂow rate of the bubble is measured by an infrared sensor and is
displayed on a screen. The measurement is repeated a number of times, giving an average
ﬂow rate, which have been used as the calibrated ﬂow rate. This has been done for all
ﬂows prior to the ﬁrst experiment with a given ﬂow setting. The ﬂow accuracy of the
calibration system is ±1% of the measured ﬂow [58].
43
==PAGE:56==
3.3
Calibration of reactor temperature
3.3
Calibration of reactor temperature
From the literature study, it is known that the SNCR process is very temperature de-
pendent. Thus, in order to get reliable experimental data, it is important to know the
temperature of the reactor and to have a uniform temperature proﬁle through the reactor.
In order to ensure that the temperature in the reactor was known, and that the temper-
ature proﬁle throughout the reactor was approximately uniform during experiments, the
temperature of the reactor oven has been calibrated during this project prior to exper-
imental work. During the calibration three thermocouples, with an uncertainty of ±2.5
K, were placed at diﬀerent positions in the reactor for temperature measurements. The
thermocouples were inserted from the top of the reactor. One was placed in the top part,
one in the center and the last one in the bottom part of the reactor. The heating elements
of the oven were turned on and set to the desired reactor temperature. When the temper-
ature measurements were steady, the set points of the individual heating elements were
adjusted until steady temperature measurements were obtained at the desired reactor
temperature. The measured temperature at each thermocouple position was noted along
with the heat element settings. Subsequently, the thermocouple placed in the reactor
bottom was pulled up through the reactor at a slow, constant rate while the temperature
was logged, in order to illustrate the temperature proﬁle throughout the reactor. Finally,
the thermocouples were reinserted and the procedure was repeated for diﬀerent temper-
atures. The experiments have been performed for each temperature between 973 and
1173 K with increments of 50 K, and 25 K increments just around the optimum temper-
ature. However, as the calibration was time consuming it has only been performed in the
temperature range with increments of 50 K. For temperatures in between, interpolation
have been used in order to get the settings for the heating elements. It was desired to
complete the experiments up to a temperature of 1273 K. However, the experiment at
1223 K and 1273 K had to be cancelled due to insuﬃcient heating power of the bottom
heating element. The measured temperatures at each thermocouple position along with
the settings of the heating elements and the desired temperature is given in Table 4.
Table 4: Temperature conditions and heat element settings from temperature calibration.

The temperature proﬁle from the bottom of the reactor to the top of the reactor, found
by slowly pulling up the bottom thermocouple through the reactor, is shown for a desired
temperature of 973 K in Figure 26. It is seen that the reactor is not completely isothermal,
as the temperature ﬂuctuates approximately ±6 K from the average temperature. Two
44
==PAGE:57==
3.3
Calibration of reactor temperature
peaks are observed, which seems to be located right between two heating elements. The
ﬁrst peak between the bottom and center heating elements and the second peak between
the middle and top heating elements. A reason for having temperature peaks at these
locations could be, that the heating eﬀects of two heating elements are combined and
therefore the temperature is higher. At the locations right next to the separate heating
elements, only the heating eﬀects of a single heating element aﬀect the temperature, which
as a consequence is slightly lower. The general form of the temperature proﬁle is similar
for the other temperatures. However, the ﬂuctuations increase slightly with increasing
temperature. The temperature proﬁles for the rest of the calibrated temperatures can be
found in Appendix 7.2.1. The distance from the bottom of the reactor, in Figure 26, is an
approximation based on the assumption, that the thermocouple has been pulled through
the reactor at a constant rate. Thus, there is some uncertainty of the exact distance from
the bottom of the reactor.
Figure 26: Temperature proﬁle throughout the reactor, from bottom to top, at the desired
temperature of 973 K.
Even though some ﬂuctuations of the temperature were observed throughout the reactor,
the reactor has been considered isothermal during the processing and model simulation
of data in the later stages of the report. The length of the isothermal zone has been
approximated as 45 cm. When processing the experimental data, the average temperature
of the reactor in the ’isothermal’ zone has been used instead of the desired temperature.
The average temperature along with the diﬀerence from the average, both above and
below, within the zone is listed for each desired temperature in Table 5.

3.4
Kinetic model
In this section the kinetic model, which in later parts of the report is used to compare to
experimental results, is presented.
3.4.1
Initial model
The initial model of this project is constituted by a large subset of nitrogen chemistry,
including an NH3 subset [44], an amine subset etc. [44], an H2/O2 subset [44], an H2S/O2
subset [59] and sulfur/nitrogen interactions [60].
Some of the most important reactions of the NH3 subset and H2/O2 subset have been
discussed previously in section 2.2.9 of the literature study, in the description of detailed
SNCR chemistry. These reactions are re-summarized in Table 6.
Besides these reactions, some of the important reactions of the H2S/O2 subset and sul-
fur/nitrogen interactions are introduced here. Dagaut and Nicolle suggested that some of
the most important reactions involving SO2 are [5]:
SO2 + O (+M) ⇌SO3 (+M)
(R 3.1)
SO2 + H (+M) ⇌HOSO (+M)
(R 3.2)
HOSO + O2 ⇌HO2 + SO2
(R 3.3)
Some of the nitrogen/sulfur interactions used in the model include:
SO2 + N ⇌NO + SO
(R 3.4)
SO2 + NH ⇌HNO + SO
(R 3.5)
SO2 + NO2 ⇌SO3 + NO
(R 3.6)
The full model is comprehensive and will not be included in the main report. It can
instead be found in the Appendix 7.6. The reactions of the initial model, which have
been considered important above, are summarized along with their reaction kinetics in
Table 6.

Decomposition of ammonium sulfate
In order to model the reduction of NO by ammonium sulfate, the thermal decomposition
of ammonium sulfate has to be taken into account. During initial modelling, the time used
for ammonium sulfate decomposition has been neglected, and therefore the decomposition
reaction itself was not added to the initial model. Instead, the expected products of NH3
and SO2 from ammonium sulfate decomposition have been considered as initial conditions.
The expected NH3 and SO2 yields of the decomposition have been found using the thermal
decomposition reaction suggested by Halstead [46]:
3 (NH4)2SO4 −−→4 NH3 + 3 SO2 + 6 H2O + N2
(R 3.7)
The reaction was proposed from the results of experiments where dried ammonium sulfate
were rapidly heated to 673 K. The gaseous products were ﬂushed into two Drechsel bottles,
in which the products were dissolved in diﬀerent reagents [46]. These diﬀerent reagents
were chosen as to detect ammonia, SO2 and SO3.
Furthermore, the remaining solid
47
==PAGE:60==
3.4
Kinetic model
residues were analyzed for ammonia and sulfate contents. For a more detailed description
of the analysis method, see section 2.3. As the experiments of Halstead were performed at
a signiﬁcantly lower temperature (673 K) than the experiments of this project (973−1173
K), there may be some uncertainty as to the applicability of the decomposition product
yields from the results of Halstead to the decomposition reaction occurring during the
experiments of this project. The use of reaction R 3.7 is discussed and evaluated at later
stages of the report and a modiﬁed approach on how to model the thermal decomposition
of ammonium sulfate has been included in the ﬁnal model.
3.4.2
Model modiﬁcations
In an attempt to improve the model, some modiﬁcations have been made. These modiﬁ-
cations will be reviewed in this section. The modiﬁcations have been made on the basis
of the experimental results and literature.
Initial reaction kinetics of ammonium sulfate decomposition
In the initial model, the decomposition time of ammonium sulfate was neglected, as it
was assumed to occur instantaneously. However, in order to test this assumption, the
ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction R 3.7 has been added to the kinetic model
with the decomposition kinetics found by Wu et al. [47]. The kinetics of R 3.7 can be
found in Table 7.

Introduction of NH2SO2 subset
In order to consider the inhibiting eﬀect of SO2 on reduction of NO, the initial model
has been modiﬁed. Dagaut and Nicolle have proposed the reaction between NH2 and
SO2 producing NH2SO2, as an important reaction for the interaction between nitrogen
compounds and SO2 [5].
The reaction kinetics of amino radicals with SO2 has been
studied by Gao et al. [66]. Therefore, an NH2SO2 subset has been included in the model.
The reactions considered are:
NH2 + SO2( + M) ⇌NH2SO2( + M)
(R 3.8)
NH2SO2 + O2 ⇌NHSO2 + HO2
(R 3.9)
NH2SO2 + H ⇌NHSO2 + H2
(R 3.10)
NH2SO2 + O ⇌NHSO2 + OH
(R 3.11)
NH2SO2 + OH ⇌NHSO2 + H2O
(R 3.12)

NH2SO2 + HO2 ⇌NHSO2 + H2O2
(R 3.13)
NH + SO2( + M) ⇌NHSO2( + M)
(R 3.14)
The kinetics of reaction R 3.8 and reaction R 3.9 have been determined by Gao et al. [66].
Reactions R 3.10−R 3.13 have been proposed under the assumption, that the NH2SO2 +
radical reactions are analogous to the corresponding NH3 + radical reactions. Therefore,
the reaction kinetics for the NH3 + radical reactions have been used for the corresponding
NH2SO2 + radical reactions. In a similar way, it has been assumed that reaction R 3.14
is analogous to SO2 + O( + M) ⇌SO3( + M). The kinetics of the NH2SO2 subset can be

Alternative ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction
In the initial model, it was assumed that all sulfur was released as SO2 during thermal
decomposition of ammonium sulfate, as proposed by Halstead [46]. However as previously
described in section 2.3 of the literature study, Wu et al. [47] suggested that the sulfur
is released as a mixture of SO2 and SO3 during thermal decomposition of ammonium
sulfate, and the yield of SO2 is almost linearly increasing with increasing temperature.
No SO3 has been detected during the experiments of this project. However, the SO2
concentration in the outlet gas has been found to increase with increasing temperatures.
Thus, the results support the temperature dependent relationship of SO2 and SO3 sug-
gested by Wu et al. [47]. It is furthermore assumed, that the reason no SO3 has been
detected during the experiments of this project is, that all SO3 produced during ammo-
nium sulfate decomposition is consumed downstream the reactor, possibly via reactions
with NH3 or water at the lower temperatures between the reactor and the FTIR.
The decomposition reaction suggested by Wu et al. is [47]:
(NH4)2SO4 −−→2 NH3 + H2O + x SO2 + (1−x) SO3 + x
2 O2
(R 3.15)
49
==PAGE:62==
3.5
Experimental procedure
However, this reaction produces more NH3 than reaction R 3.7 suggested by Halstead [46].
The results of the coming sections and unpublished studies by Krum [67] indicate that
the amount of NH3 produced from ammonium sulfate decomposition is lower than that
suggested by Halstead [46], and thereby signiﬁcantly lower than that suggested by Wu
et al. [47]. Furthermore, the ammonia yield was not measured during the experiments
of Wu et al. [47], but only the SO2 yield. Therefore, reaction R 3.15 is slightly modiﬁed
in the model in order to adjust the amount of NH3 to a lower value, in consistency with
experiment values determined by [67], while maintaining the amount of SO2 and SO3.
Reaction R 3.15 has been rewritten into two parallel reactions, in order to take into account
the inﬂuences of decomposition temperature on the production of SO2/SO3 and NH3 in
Chemkin:
(NH4)2SO4 −−→2 NH3 + H2O + SO3
(R 3.16)
(NH4)2SO4 −−→4
3 NH3 + 2 H2O + SO2 + 1
3 N2
(R 3.17)
Reaction R 3.15 was in the study of Wu et al. [47] realized in Chemkin by reaction R 3.16
and (NH4)2SO4 −−→2 NH3 +H2O+SO2 + 1
2 O2 with the reaction kinetics found in Table
9. The kinetics are valid for T = 873 −1173 K [47]. In this project, the second reaction
has been modiﬁed to reaction R 3.17 with the same kinetics, in order to adjust for the
high yield of NH3.
Table 9:
Kinetics of modiﬁed ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction.
The
given parameters can be used in a modiﬁed Arrhenius expression of the form k =

No further model modiﬁcations have been made during this project.
3.5
Experimental procedure
After testing of the setup for problems, solving of the essential problems and calibration
of gas ﬂows and oven temperature, the main experiments have been performed.
3.5.1
Thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate
As the literature showed some discrepancies regarding the decomposition products of
ammonium sulfate, it was desired to experimentally investigate the thermal decomposition
of ammonium sulfate, using the setup developed during this project.
Initially, the reactor was heated to the desired temperature using the oven settings listed
in Table 4. Subsequently, the heat tracing of the outlet gas was turned on and adjusted.
50
==PAGE:63==
3.5
Experimental procedure
Upon reaching the desired reactor and heat tracing temperature, a 0.5 NL/min N2 carrier
ﬂow was turned on along with the mist generator. Even though a signiﬁcant amount of
mist entered the reactor, observed by an increase in water concentration, no NH3 or SO3
was detected in the outlet gas from the FTIR measurements and only small amounts of
SO2 were detected. No change in the outlet gas composition was observed at varying
temperatures and a pressure buildup was observed after a couple of hours of experiments.
Due to the pressure buildup, the setup had to be shut down and the experiments stopped.
As all of the literature regarding ammonium sulfate decomposition reported NH3 as one
of the main products along with SO2 and/or SO3, it was assumed that the NH3, SO2
and/or SO3 formed during the decomposition process were ’lost’ downstream the reactor.
A hypothesis is, that ammonium sulfate or a similar compound is reformed in the tubing
between the reactor and the FTIR. This would also explain the pressure buildup, as the
particles could clog the ﬁlter.
No solutions were found to the challenge. Thus, the exact decomposition process was
not experimentally investigated during this project, and the quantity of diﬀerent decom-
position product species had to estimated from assumptions using the ﬁndings of the
literature.
3.5.2
SNCR using ammonium sulfate
The experiments were started by heating the reactor to a desired temperature, using
the oven settings listed in Table 4, and turning on heat tracing of the outlet gas. The
gas ﬂows were then adjusted in order to obtain the desired inlet gas composition. Upon
reaching a steady state at the desired reactor temperature, heat tracing temperature and
inlet composition, the mist generator was turned on. The outlet gas composition was
measured by the FTIR and the data was logged. In the beginning, the mist feed to the
reactor increased signiﬁcantly. However, after some time the mist feed almost stabilized
with only a small increase over time. At this point the NO concentration reached a steady
state. The NO concentration of the steady state was noted. Subsequently, the reactor
temperature was changed and the NO concentration was noted upon reaching a new steady
state. This procedure was repeated using temperatures within the reduction temperature
window. At the end of each experiment, the system was ﬂushed with nitrogen.
The feeding rate of the ammonium sulfate mist was determined from calculations based
on the water concentration in the outlet gas, measured by the FTIR, and the composition
of the ammonium sulfate solution. The calculations can be found in Appendix 7.5.
During the experiments, the pressure inside the system increased gradually, as the ﬁlter
was partly clogged. Therefore, the ﬁlter was removed and changed at the end of each
experiment in order to avoid pressure buildup in the system, and consequences thereof.
The dirty ﬁlters were cleaned by demineralized water subjected to an ultrasonic bath and
re-used.
Diﬀerent molar ratios of ammonium sulfate to NO have been investigated during the
51
==PAGE:64==
3.5
Experimental procedure
project and the ratio was adjusted by preparing ammonium sulfate solutions with varying
concentrations. The varying ammonium sulfate solutions were interchanged in the mist
tank, and thus diﬀerent molar ratios were investigated using the same inlet concentration
of NO.
3.5.3
Gas reference SNCR experiments using NH3
Reference gas experiments using NH3 gas as reducing agent have been used to investigate
the inﬂuence of SO2 on the SNCR process in the temperature range of the experiments.
Furthermore, they have been compared to the experiments using ammonium sulfate so-
lution as reducing agent.
The experimental procedure for SNCR using ammonia gas as the reducing agent was
similar to that of using ammonium sulfate as reducing agent.
The diﬀerence was that the mist tank contained demineralized water instead of aqueous
ammonium sulfate solution and that the ammonia used for reduction of NO was intro-
duced as a gas ﬂow instead of originating from thermal decomposition of ammonium
sulfate.
Again the reactor was heated to the desired temperature, while the heat tracing of the
outlet gas was turned on. The gas ﬂows, with the additional NH3 gas ﬂow, were adjusted
to obtain the desired inlet gas composition with an initial NH3 concentration similar to
that expected for the experiments using ammonium sulfate solution. The mist generator
was again turned on upon reaching a steady state at the desired reactor temperature, heat
tracing temperature and inlet composition, in order to obtain a similar concentration of
water vapor in the system. The water concentration in the system was measured by FTIR.
The steady state concentration of NO was then noted at diﬀerent temperatures within
the reduction temperature window.
The experiments were performed both in the presence and absence of SO2. Initially, the
experiments were performed without any SO2 in the system. Subsequently, the experi-
ments were performed in the presence of SO2 in the system. The amount of SO2 added was
similar to that expected during the experiments using ammonium sulfate solution. The
expected concentration of both NH3 and SO2 was found using the thermal decomposition
reaction of Halstead [46] described in section 2.3 of the literature study:
3 (NH4)2SO4 −−→4 NH3 + 3 SO2 + 6 H2O + N2
Using the outlet concentration of water vapor in the system, the expected inlet concen-
tration of NH3 and SO2 has been calculated. The calculations can be found in Appendix
7.5.
The gas reference experiments were only performed for one initial ratio of NH3 to NO and
for one oxygen concentration of the system.
52
==PAGE:65==
3.5
Experimental procedure
3.5.4
Analysis of ﬁlter clogging particles
In order to investigate the composition of the particles, which were formed and found
to clog the ﬁlter prior to the FTIR, small samples were collected and analyzed using a
Scanning Electron Microscope, also known as SEM-EDX.
The particles were collected by cleaning the ﬁlter in demineralized water subjected to
an ultrasonic bath. The particle containing water was then transferred to an aluminum
cup and put in an oven at 383 K to evaporate the water. The dried particles were then
transferred to a carbon tape and SEM-EDX analysis of the samples was performed.
53
==PAGE:66==
4
Results and discussion
In this section the results of the experimental work will be presented, compared to mod-
elling work and the essential ﬁndings will be discussed.
The initial conditions used during experiments can be found in Table 10. The experimental
conditions have been varied, such that the eﬀect of the oxygen concentration and the
initial molar ratio of (NH4)2SO4/NO can be found by comparison of the experimental
results. Furthermore, reference experiments directly using NH3 and SO2 gases have been
performed in order to compare the reduction performance using ammonium sulfate and
NH3, and to test the eﬀect of SO2 on the SNCR process.

The initial concentration of NO2 is estimated, assuming that it is the same for all cases
with approximately the same oxygen concentration. The NO2 is formed in the top piece,
which is mounted on top of the reactor. Here the NO and O2 is mixed below the reactor
temperature, and as a consequence they react to form a relatively small amount NO2. For
both the oxygen settings, the initial concentration of NO2 has been estimated by lowering
the reactor temperature until the outlet NO2 concentration stabilized in the experiment
with low AS. The value at which a steady state was achieved, even as the temperature was
decreased, has been used as the initial concentration for all experiments with a similar
oxygen concentration.
The outlet concentration of NO and NO2 used to generate the plots of the upcoming
sections has been found by taking the average value of the data logged by the FTIR over
a period of time, which has been considered as a steady state.
4.1
Experimental results
4.1.1
Composition of ﬁlter clogging particles
The particles, which were found to be formed during experiments and consequently
clogged the ﬁlter, causing pressure buildup, have been analyzed using SEM-EDX. Two
samples have been investigated.
The average weight concentration of each elements in the samples are listed in Table 13.
The SEM-EDX analysis has been performed at several diﬀerent locations of the samples,
and the listed results are the overall average values.
The data used to generate the
table can be found in the Appendix 7.4. There is a signiﬁcant variation in the weight
concentration for the diﬀerent locations.
However, the main objective of the analysis
has been to obtain a qualitative determination of the atoms, of which the particles are
composed.
The results show that the samples contained large amounts of O atoms along with Fe,
S and Cr. For sample 1, Al was detected as well. The analysis also indicated that the
56
==PAGE:69==
4.1
Experimental results
samples contained H, N and C atoms. However, the measurements of these atoms are
not considered reliable using the SEM-EDX used in this project and have therefore been
omitted.
The Al detected during analysis of sample 1 is residues from the aluminum sample holder.
Sample 2 was contained in a plastic cup and did not show any detection of Al. Otherwise,
sample 1 and 2 have been collected using the same method. However, sample 1 had a
more distinct brownish color compared to sample 2, which had a slightly yellow color.
The composition of the two samples diﬀered as well. Sample 1 contained more Fe relative
to S, while sample 2 contained more S relative to Fe.
The presence of Fe and Cr atoms along with a brownish appearance of the particles for
sample 1, indicate that the particles, at least partially, are composed of rust. The rust
is probably formed at the surface of the ﬁlter, which is made from stainless steel. This
indicates that some corrosive species are formed in the outlet tubing downstream of the
reactor. One possibility is the formation of sulfuric acid due to the presence of water
vapor and SO3.
The presence of sulfur atoms may indicate, that some ammonium sulfate or a similar
compound are reformed downstream the reactor. The potential formation of ammonium
sulfate downstream, could explain why no ammonia and SO3, and only little SO2, relative
to the expected amount, is measured in the outlet gas by the FTIR.
Even though the samples do not have the same concentration of the diﬀerent atoms, they
indicate that the particles consist of a mixture between rust and some sulfur containing
compound, which may be reformed ammonium sulfate.

results have been obtained by SEM-EDX analysis.

The amount of particles collected were not suﬃcient to perform other types of analysis,
such as TGA or XRD, which could have given more conclusive results regarding the

4.1.2
Eﬀect of AS/NO molar ratio on NO reduction
The eﬀect of the molar ratio of initial ammonium sulfate to NO on the reduction of
NO has been investigated by comparing experiments with similar oxygen concentration
and varying molar ratios of AS/NO. Three variations of AS/NO have been compared at
two diﬀerent oxygen concentrations. The results are depicted in Figure 28 and Figure
29, which are at a low (1.25 −1.26%) and high (8.06 −8.19%) oxygen concentration
respectively.
57
==PAGE:70==
4.1
Experimental results
Figure 28 shows the results of NO reduction for varying initial ratios of ammonium sulfate
to NO at oxygen levels of 1.25 −1.26%. From the experimental results it is seen that the
optimum temperature for NO reduction in the case with a molar ratio of AS/NO = 0.38 is
approximately 1121 K. At this temperature, a fraction of 0.82 of the initial NO is present at
the reactor exit. With a ratio of AS/NO = 0.77 the fraction of remaining NO is 0.73 at the
approximate optimum temperature of 1121 K, while the fraction of remaining NO has been
found to 0.47 at an experimental optimum temperature of 1144 K for the case of initial
AS/NO = 1.62. At the lower end of the temperature range investigated, the reduction
eﬃciency is similar for the three varying AS/NO ratios investigated. This applies up to a
temperature of approximately 1072 K. However, as the optimum approaches, the reduction
eﬃciency increases and an increased diﬀerence in the reduction eﬃciency for the varying
ratios is seen. At temperatures above the observed optimum, the NO reduction eﬃciency
decrease in a similar fashion for all initial ratios.

’Medium AS, low O2’ (AS/NO = 0.77) - (NH4)2SO4=286 ppm, NO=370 ppm, O2=1.25%,
H2O=4.04%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6432/T s.
’High AS, low O2’ (AS/NO = 1.62) - (NH4)2SO4=604 ppm, NO=373 ppm, O2=1.25%,
H2O=4.11%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6427/T s.
T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
Similarly, the results of NO reduction for varying initial ratios of ammonium sulfate to
NO at oxygen levels of 8.06 −8.19% are depicted in Figure 29. Here it is seen, that the
58
==PAGE:71==
4.1
Experimental results
observed optimum in case with a molar ratio of AS/NO = 0.41 is located at a temperature
of 1031 K. At this temperature, the fraction of NO at the reactor exit has been found
as 0.84. For AS/NO = 0.77 the optimum temperature has been found as 1045 K with
NOﬁnal/NOinitial = 0.80. Finally, the minimum fraction of NO remaining after the reactor
has been found to 0.63 at a temperature of 1045 K for initial AS/NO = 1.98. Here,
the optimum reduction is observed in the low temperature range, while the reduction
eﬃciency slowly decreases for temperature increases above the optimum. This applies for
all three cases of varying molar ratios.

The results of Figure 28 and Figure 29 show, that an increased reduction of NO is achieved
in the temperature range investigated, when increasing the molar ratio of AS/NO. This
applies for both conditions of the oxygen concentration. This conforms to the trend seen in
the results of the literature discussed in the literature study, where an increasing amount
of reducing agent to NO, improves reduction.
Furthermore, it is seen that the temperature window for eﬀective NO reduction is located
in approximately the same temperature range for varying AS/NO ratios at a similar
59
==PAGE:72==
4.1
Experimental results
oxygen concentration. However, the results indicate that the temperature of optimum
reduction is shifted slightly towards higher temperatures for increasing ratio of AS/NO.
When comparing the experimental results of this project to SNCR data of the literature
using similar laboratory-scale ﬂow reactors, it is seen that the reduction eﬃciency of
ammonium sulfate is signiﬁcantly lower than that of NH3. Furthermore, it is seen, that
the temperature window of eﬀective NO reduction is located at lower temperatures for this
study relative to literature data. Here, the experimental data of this project is compared
to the studies of Liang et al. [27], Lu and Lu [28] and Duo et al. [32], which have all used
laboratory-scale ﬂow reactors. Furthermore, all of these studies have been described in
the literature study.
The NO reduction is initiated at temperatures around 1000 K for the low oxygen con-
ditions (∼1.25%) and around 950 K for the high oxygen conditions (∼8.10%) of this
project. This is low compared to data found in the literature, where the reduction of NO
by NH3 is initiated at temperatures of around ∼1050 −1070 K [27, 28, 32]. The liter-
ature data is obtained using a variety of oxygen concentration, which includes both the
low and high oxygen concentration used in this project. However, the literature data has
been obtained using NH3 as reducing agent instead of ammonium sulfate in this project.
Furthermore, the optimum temperature for NO reduction has been found to be between
1121 −1144 K for the cases of low oxygen and 1031 −1045 K for the cases of high oxygen
used in this project. In the literature, which has been used for comparison, the optimum
temperature varies between ∼1170 −1240 K [27, 28, 32]. The literature data indicates,
that the residence time has an eﬀect on the temperature window. The study of Duo et
al. [32], where the temperature window is located at highest temperatures (initiated at
∼1070K with an optimum of ∼1240K), has used a gas residence time of 93.3/T s. On
the other hand, Liang et al. [27], where the temperature window is located at lowest
temperatures (initiated at ∼1050K with an optimum of ∼1170K), used a residence time
of 1024/T s. Thus, it is indicated that prolonging the gas residence time shifts the tem-
perature window towards lower temperatures. This was also one of the ﬁndings of the
literature study. As the experiments of this project was performed using a signiﬁcantly
longer residence time (above 6300/T s for all experiments), this might explain the location
of the temperature window at relatively low temperatures in this project.
The lowest fraction of NOﬁnal/NOinitial, obtained using ammonium sulfate as reducing
agent, during this project was 0.47. This value is high compared to ∼0.06 [27], ∼0.10
[28] and ∼0.15 [32] found in the literature. Thus, the reduction eﬃciency obtained during
this project is signiﬁcantly lower than found in the literature. The initial molar ratio of
NH3/NO varies from 1.20 −1.64 in the literature studies [27, 28, 32]. Thereby it is lower
than or approximately the same as the initial molar ratio of AS/NO = 1.62, which is used
for the most eﬃcient ammonium sulfate reduction experiment of this project. The NO
reduction has been obtained using NH3 in the literature, whereas ammonium sulfate has
been used during this project. The results seem to indicate, that ammonium sulfate is not
as eﬃcient a reducing agent as NH3. However, the low temperatures of this project may
aﬀect the maximum reduction. Furthermore, the presence of SO2/SO3 in the experiments
60
==PAGE:73==
4.1
Experimental results
of this project may have an inﬂuence on the NO reduction.
4.1.3
Eﬀect of O2 on NO reduction
By comparing the results of experiments with similar initial molar ratio of AS/NO and
varying O2 concentration, the eﬀect of O2 on NO reduction has been investigated. Two
variations of oxygen concentration have been compared at three diﬀerent AS/NO molar
ratios. Figure 30, Figure 31 and Figure 32, with low (0.38 −0.41), medium (0.77) and
high (1.62 −1.98) AS/NO ratio respectively, show the results.
Figure 30 shows the NO reductions for the cases of low initial ammonium sulfate to NO
at low and high oxygen concentrations. The results show, that the NO reduction occurs
at low temperatures at an oxygen concentration of 8.06% (AS/NO = 0.41), with an the
optimum temperature of 1031 K and NOﬁnal/NOinitial = 0.84. At temperatures above
the optimum, the reduction eﬃciency slightly decreases with increasing temperature. At
an oxygen concentration of 1.26% (AS/NO = 0.38), the reduction is less eﬃcient in
the lower temperature range. The reduction is improved as the temperature approaches
the optimum of 1121 K, where a fraction of 0.82 of the initial NO was detected in the
outlet gas. At temperatures above the optimum, the reduction eﬃciency rapidly decrease
compared to the case of high oxygen concentration.

high oxygen concentrations are shown in Figure 31. The trends are similar to those from
Figure 30. At a high oxygen concentration of 8.09%, the NO reduction eﬃciency increase
rapidly for increasing temperatures in the lower temperature range, until the optimum is
reached at 1045 K. A fraction of 0.80 of the initial NO is present in the outlet gas at this
temperature. As for the case at low initial AS/NO and high O2, the reduction eﬃciency
is slightly decreased with increasing temperatures above the optimum. At the low O2
concentration of 1.25%, the reduction is relatively slowly increasing for the temperature
increase from 975−1017 K. At the higher temperatures, the increase of reduction eﬃciency
with increasing temperatures is ampliﬁed until reaching the optimum temperature of 1121
K. The fraction of NOﬁnal/NOinitial at 1121 K is 0.73. Relative to the case of high oxygen,
the reduction is worsened fast with increasing temperatures above the optimum.

approaches, the increase of reduction eﬃciency with increasing temperature is ampliﬁed.

All of the results show that the temperature window is shifted towards higher temperatures
and the maximum reduction is improved by lowering the oxygen concentration. Both
these trends conform to the results found and discussed during the literature study. It is
furthermore seen, that the improvement in maximum reduction is increasing for increasing
molar ratios of AS/NO in the range investigated.
Furthermore, the results indicate that the temperature window is widened when the
oxygen level is increased. This was also one of the ﬁndings of the literature study.
4.1.4
NO2 outlet concentration
Due to mixing of NO and O2 in the reactor top piece, at signiﬁcantly lower temperatures
than the reactor temperature, NO2 is formed in relatively small amounts. Therefore, it
has been of interest to consider the NO2 outlet concentration as well.
Figure 33 shows the NO2 outlet concentrations for the case of medium molar ratio of
AS/NO = 0.77 in two varying oxygen concentrations. Figure 34 shows the outlet concen-
tration of NO2 for the case of low oxygen (1.25 −1.26%) in three varying initial AS/NO
molar ratios. More results at diﬀerent conditions have been prepared. However, as the
64
==PAGE:77==
4.1
Experimental results
reduction of NO2 has not been one of the main focuses of the project, these have been
moved to Appendix 7.2.2.
From the results of Figure 33 it is seen, that the NO2 outlet concentration is generally
decreasing with increasing temperature at both low and high oxygen concentrations. The
lowest temperature of 975 K is a deviating point for the case of high oxygen concentration
at 8.09%.
Here the outlet concentration of NO2 was found to be 22.0 ppm, while it
increases to 31.6 ppm at 1017 K. From here it was found to step-wise decrease with
increasing temperature ending up at 6.3 ppm at the highest temperature of 1167 K. For
the case of low O2 at 1.25%, the NO2 outlet concentration is step-wise decreasing with
increasing temperatures over the whole temperature range investigated. It has been found
as 22.4 ppm at the lowest temperature of 975 K and decreases to 2.4 ppm at the highest
temperature of 1167 K. Both at the low and high oxygen concentration, the decrease with
increasing temperature is generally more signiﬁcant in the lower temperature end.

’Medium AS, low O2’ (AS/NO = 0.77) - (NH4)2SO4=286 ppm, NO=370 ppm, O2=1.25%,
H2O=4.04%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6432/T s.
’Medium AS, high O2’ (AS/NO

O2=8.09%, H2O=3.93%, NO2=40.4 ppm and residence time of 6446/T s.

Figure 34 shows very similar NO2 outlet concentration for the cases of low AS/NO (0.38)
and medium AS/NO (0.77) at all temperatures. At the lowest temperature of 975 K,
the outlet concentrations were 23.2 and 22.4 ppm at low AS/NO and medium AS/NO
65
==PAGE:78==

Experimental results
conditions respectively. For both cases, the NO2 at the outlet decreases similarly with
increasing temperatures.
The outlet concentrations were 1.6 ppm for the case of low
AS/NO and 2.4 for the case of medium AS/NO at the highest temperature of 1167 K.
The levels of NO2 is signiﬁcantly lower in the low temperature end for the case of high
NO/AS of 1.62. At 975 K the NO2 outlet concentration was 4.5. It decreases to 0.7 ppm
at 1072 K, after which is increase to 1.8 ppm at 1121 K. Finally, it decreases to 0.9 ppm

The general trend of the NO2 outlet concentration, seen from Figure 33 and Figure 34,
is, that it seems to decrease with increasing temperature. This is the case for the vast
majority of the results with only a few deviating data points throughout all of the exper-
iments. These deviations may be the result of some source of error in the measurement
or conditions, some uncertainty or some deviating behavior at these exact conditions. It
might be caused by diﬀerent properties of NO2 formation/reduction at the conditions of
these data points.
Figure 33 shows that the outlet concentration of NO2 is lower at low oxygen conditions.
66
==PAGE:79==
4.1
Experimental results
The NO2 is formed prior to the reactor, as NO and O2 is mixed. At conditions with
less oxygen, the amount of NO2 formed is lower. Thus, it is reasonable, that the out-
let concentration of NO2 is lower in this case. However, the data point at the lowest
temperature deviates from the trend. This point also deviates from the general trend of
increasing concentration with decreasing temperature at high oxygen conditions. Thus,
this is assessed to be a faulty data point.
Figure 34 does not show any conclusive trends of the eﬀect of varying the initial am-
monium sulfate concentration. Even though the concentrations are clearly lower for the
high AS concentration, no clear distinction is seen from the cases of low to medium AS
concentration.
The observations on the NO2 outlet concentration are generally supported by the addi-
tional results of NO2 reduction found in the appendices.
4.1.5
Gas reference experiments
Figure 35 shows the reduction of NO, and Figure 36 shows the NO2 outlet concentration,
using ammonium sulfate mist compared to ammonia gas as reducing agent. The experi-
ments using ammonia gas were performed both in the absence of SO2 and in the presence
of SO2 in a concentration similar to that expected from decomposition of ammonium
sulfate.
The results of Figure 35 show a similar optimum temperature and form for the NO
reduction using ammonium sulfate and NH3 gas as reducing agent. The case of ammonium
sulfate has an initial molar ratio of AS/NO of 0.77 and an oxygen concentration of 1.25%.
The cases of NH3 gas, both with and without SO2, have an O2 concentration of 1.26%.
The initial molar ratio of NH3/NO is 1.06 and 1.05 in the gas reference experiments with
and without SO2 respectively. From the experimental results, it is seen that the optimum
temperature for NO reduction in the case using ammonium sulfate is approximately 1121
K. At this temperature, a fraction of 0.73 of the initial NO is present at the reactor
exit.
The NO reduction has its optimum temperature of 1144 K for the cases using
NH3 gas as reducing agent, both in the absence and presence of SO2. At the optimum
temperature, the fraction of outlet NO to inlet NO is 0.50 and 0.27 with and without SO2
respectively. No or very slight NO reduction is observed at temperatures of 975 and 1017
K using ammonium sulfate, after which the reduction is improved more drastically with
increasing temperatures towards the optimum. At temperatures above the optimum, the
decrease in reduction eﬃciency with increasing temperatures is relatively small compared
to changes just below the optimum. Using NH3 gas, the NO reduction is more eﬃcient
at the low temperature end as well. At a temperature of 975 K the outlet NO to inlet
NO was found as 0.91 and 0.94 with and without SO2 respectively. As for the case of
ammonium sulfate, the reduction is slightly improved with increasing temperatures up to
1072 K, both with and without SO2. With a temperature increase, from 1072 to 1121 K,
the reduction is signiﬁcantly improved for both cases. However, the largest improvement
is observed for the case without SO2. A further increase of temperature slightly improves
67
==PAGE:80==
4.1
Experimental results
the reduction to its maximum, after which a slight decrease in NO reduction eﬃciency is
observed for increasing temperatures.

temperature of 1167 K was 2.4 ppm. No signiﬁcant amounts of outlet NO2 was observed

with and without SO2.

==PAGE:81==
4.1
Experimental results
Figure 36: Comparison of NO2 outlet concentrations using ammonium sulfate mist and
NH3 gas as reducing agent, and eﬀect of SO2 in low O2 conditions. Average initial condi-

SO2=0 ppm, H2O=3.24%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6493/T s.
’Gas reference, SO2’ (NH3/NO = 1.06) - NH3=392 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%,
SO2=289 ppm, H2O=3.16%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6501/T s.
T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
The results in Figure 35 show a clear eﬀect of SO2 on the NO reduction. From a com-
parison of the gas reference experiments, it is seen that SO2 signiﬁcantly inhibits the
reduction of NO in the temperature range around the optimum. The studies of Dagaut
and Nicolle [5] and Silver [34], discussed in the literature study (section 2.2.7), did not
show any inhibiting eﬀect of SO2 on the maximum reduction of NO. Instead, they found
that the optimum temperature was shifted approximately 30 K towards higher tempera-
tures. Thus, the experimental ﬁndings of this project deviates from the studies of Dagaut
and Nicolle [5] and Silver [34]. However, the ﬁndings of this project support the ﬁndings
of Li et al. [35], which are also discussed in section 2.2.7. Here, the presence of SO2
was found to have an inhibiting eﬀect on the maximum NO reduction (from 95.4% to
77.8%). The experiments by Dagaut and Nicolle [5] were conducted with a signiﬁcantly
lower residence time (100 ms) compared to the experiments of Li et al. [35] (1.2 s) and the
experiments of this project (>6300/T s, corresponding to above 5 s for all experiments).
Furthermore, Dagaut and Nicolle [5] used a jet-stirred ﬂow reactor for the experiments,
69
==PAGE:82==
4.1
Experimental results
whereas a ﬂow reactor was used in the experiments of Li et al. [35] and this project. Us-
ing the low residence time and a jet-stirred reactor, the temperature window is located at
higher temperatures for Dagaut and Nicolle [5], with an optimum temperature of ∼1270
K relative to ∼1175 K for Li et al. [35] and ∼1144 K for this project. This indicates
that the inhibiting eﬀect of SO2 is more pronounced at lower temperatures.
It is seen from Figure 35, that the reduction using ammonium sulfate mist is signiﬁcantly
worse than for the gas reference experiments. Following reaction R 3.7, the concentration
of NH3 and SO2 should be similar after ammonium sulfate decomposition, in the case
of ammonium sulfate, to the inlet concentrations used for the reference experiments.
The optimum temperature is approximately the same with only slightly higher optimum
temperatures for the gas reference experiments. From the literature study, it is seen that
the thermal decomposition may produce a mixture of SO2 and SO3 depending on the
temperature [47]. In the calculations for expected concentrations it was assumed that all
sulfur was converted to SO2. Possibly, SO3 has a more signiﬁcant inhibiting eﬀect than
SO2, which can partly explain the worsened reduction using ammonium sulfate mist. The
eﬀect of SO3 compared to SO2 is investigated using modelling results in section 4.2.1.
Another possible explanation could be, that the amount of NH3 formed during decom-
position is lower than expected in the case of ammonium sulfate mist. As seen from the
results in section 4.1.2, the NO reduction is increased by increasing the initial molar ratio
of AS/NO, and thereby also the resulting amount of NH3 from decomposition. Thus, if
the amount of NH3 produced from decomposition in the case of ammonium sulfate mist is
lower than expected, the NO reduction will be lower. As seen from the results of section
4.1.2, this could also explain the slightly lower optimum temperature seen for ammonium
sulfate mist compared to the reference gas experiments.
A lower amount of NH3 in the reactor may be caused by evaporation of water from
the ammonium sulfate solution mist prior to the reactor entrance. During experiments,
the ammonium sulfate solution mist is introduced through the reactor top piece. The
temperature in the reactor top piece is signiﬁcantly higher than room temperature. Thus,
the water of the mist may have been partly, or even fully, evaporated prior to the reactor
entrance. Evaporation of the water will have left solid ammonium sulfate particles, which
could possibly stick to the glass-ware of the top piece, preventing some of the particles
from entering the reactor. During experiments, the top piece of the reactor became slightly
opaque, which indicates that some particles had adsorbed to the glass-ware.
The amount of NH3 in the reactor may also be lower than expected, if the thermal
decomposition reaction of ammonium sulfate did not produce the amount of NH3 as
reported in the literature. The expected NH3 concentration has been calculated using the
decomposition reaction suggested by Halstead [46]. The reaction and its origin has been
described in the literature study (section 2.3) and in the introduction of the model (section
3.4). See these sections for detailed information regarding reaction R 3.7 and the use of it
as a measure of estimated concentrations of NH3 and SO2. To verify the decomposition
reaction, Krum performed preliminary transient experiments on thermal decomposition
70
==PAGE:83==
4.2
Modelling results
of solid ammonium sulfate particles in a ﬁxed bed reactor [67]. The experiments were
performed by dropping small samples of solid ammonium sulfate into a ﬁxed bed reactor
at a temperature of 1173 K. The carrier gas was 1 NL/min of N2. The outlet gas was
analyzed by an FTIR analyzer similar to that used during this project. A mass balance
using the accumulated amount of NH3 and SO2 from the decomposition experiments were
compared to reaction R 3.7 proposed by Halstead [46]. The experiments were repeated 7
times and the results showed that the amount of sulfur, in the form of SO2, was consistent
with that expected from the reaction suggested by Halstead. However, the nitrogen mass
balance was only about 80 −95% closed, suggesting a lower amount of NH3 during the
experiments. During the studies of Krum [67], it was suspected that signiﬁcant amounts
of the NH3 had adsorbed to the tubing from the reactor outlet to the FTIR. However, the
results of this project indicate that the amount of NH3 released may in fact be signiﬁcantly
lower than suggested by Halstead.
If the ammonium sulfate is not fully decomposed in the reactor, due to insuﬃcient res-
idence time, the amount of NH3 would be lower than expected as well.
This will be
discussed and evaluated in the upcoming modelling section of the report.
No clear conclusions regarding the eﬀect of SO2 on the NO2 outlet concentration can be
drawn from the experimental results presented in Figure 36. Both in conditions with
and without SO2, the NO2 is almost completely removed at all temperatures during the
reference gas experiments.
Similar to the results of NO reduction, the results of the NO2 outlet concentrations indi-
cate that the amount of produced NH3 is lower than expected using ammonium sulfate
mist, as the reduction is not nearly as eﬀective as for the reference gas. Again, a possible
explanation could also be the presence of SO3, which may have a more distinct inhibiting
eﬀect on reduction than the assumed SO2.
4.2
Modelling results
A model of the reaction kinetics has been used to simulate the reduction of NO and the
NO2 outlet concentration under the experimental conditions. The model has been in-
troduced in section 3.4. The results of the experiments and modelling, using the model
provided in section 3.4, are compared during this section of the report. The modelling
tool used during the project is Chemkin 17.1. Initially, modelling results using the initial
model presented in section 3.4.1 has been compared to the experimental results. Sub-
sequently, the modiﬁed model including the modiﬁcations presented in section 3.4.2 has
been compared to the experimental results. The reactor used for the experiments is a
ﬂow reactor and it is assumed, that it is an ideal plug ﬂow reactor. The equations, that
have to be solved in the modelling, are the same for an ideal plug ﬂow reactor and a
closed homogeneous batch reactor. Therefore, the reactor has been modelled as a closed
homogeneous batch reactor in Chemkin. The gas residence time has been determined
from the reactor dimensions and the gas volume ﬂow. The calculations can be found in
Appendix 7.5. As a simpliﬁcation, a constant gas pressure of 1.15 atm has been assumed
71
==PAGE:84==
4.2
Modelling results
for all experiments. The pressure increased from approximately 1.10−1.20 atm during
the course of one experimental day and the small variation has been neglected.
4.2.1
Initial modelling results
NO reduction
The results of the modelling using the initial model have been compared to the experi-
mental results for NO reduction in Figure 37, Figure 38 and Figure 39.
Figure 37 compares the experimental results of the reference gas experiments to the mod-
elling results. Initially, it is seen that the model predictions and the experimental results
conform to a large extent for the reference gas experiment in the absence of SO2. The
experimental optimum temperature is 1144 K with a fraction of outlet NO to inlet NO
of 0.27. The modelling results show an NOﬁnal/NOinitial of 0.14 at an optimum temper-
ature of 1123 K. Thus, in this case both the location of the temperature window and
the maximum NO reduction are predicted satisfactorily, even though the model predicts
slightly better reduction compared to the experiment. Furthermore, the model predicts
the experimental NO reduction very well in the low temperature end, and the behavior of
decreasing reduction eﬃciency with increasing temperature above optimum is very simi-
lar as well. However, the predicted reduction is better than the experimental above the
optimum.
In the case with SO2, the NO reduction has an optimum temperature of 1144 K in the
experimental results and 1123 K in the modelling results. The NOﬁnal/NOinitial has been
found as 0.50 experimentally, while it has been predicted as 0.14 by the model. Thus, the
model does not predict the inhibiting eﬀect of SO2 on NO reduction, which is found in
the experiments. The location of the temperature window is similar for the experimental
and modelling results, but the degree of reduction is signiﬁcantly higher in the modelling
results relative to the experimental results.
The model predicts only a slight eﬀect of SO2 on NO reduction in the form of a slight
narrowing of the temperature window along with slightly worsened NO reduction in the
lower temperature end, whereas the experimental results show a signiﬁcant inhibiting
eﬀect of SO2 on the maximum NO reduction.

Validity and eﬀect of assumptions
During the simulations using the initial kinetics model, some assumptions have been made.
The validity and eﬀect of the most relevant assumptions have been tested. Initially, it has
been assumed that all the sulfur from ammonium sulfate was converted to SO2 during
decomposition according to Halstead [46]. However, according to Wu et al. [47] the sulfur
will be converted to both SO2 and SO3, and the distribution is temperature dependent.
Therefore, the model has been used to test how the reduction of NO will be aﬀected if
the SO2 is partly or completely replaced by SO3. The results are shown in Figure 42.
Simulated results using the reference gas experiments without SO2 and with SO2 are
depicted along with a case where half the SO2 have been replaced by SO3 and a case
where all of the SO2 have been replaced by SO3.
It is clearly seen that the NO reduction is aﬀected by the ratio of SO2/SO3 in the system.
The maximum reduction is very similar for all cases. The minimum NOﬁnal/NOinitial has
been found as 0.14 in the case with no SO2/SO3, 0.14 in the case with SO2, 0.13 in the
case with SO3 and 0.12 in the case with half SO2 and half SO3. However, the temperature
77
==PAGE:90==
4.2
Modelling results
window is shifted towards higher temperatures by increasing the ratio of SO3/SO2. The
SO2/SO3 only aﬀects the NO reduction slightly in the lower temperature range, while it is
more pronounced around the temperature windows of eﬀective reduction. The optimum
temperature has been found from the model as 1123 K in the case with no SO2 and with
SO2. Even though the optimum temperature is the same for these cases, the temperature
window has been slightly narrowed by the addition of SO2. In the case where half the
SO2 has been replaced by SO3, the optimum temperature of the modelling results has
been found as 1153 K, while it is approximately 1173 K in the case of SO3. Thus, if
SO3 is present in the system, the assumption that all sulfur is converted to SO2 during
decomposition is a source of error. Especially in the high temperature range.
Figure 42: Eﬀect of SO2/SO3 on NO reduction in low O2 conditions using model simula-
tion. Average initial conditions for the diﬀerent experiments were:
’No SO2’ (NH3/NO = 1.05) - NH3=391 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%, SO2=0 ppm,
H2O=3.24%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6493/T s.
’SO2’ (NH3/NO = 1.06) - NH3=392 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%, SO2=289 ppm,
H2O=3.16%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6501/T s.
’SO2/SO3’ (NH3/NO = 1.06) - NH3=392 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%, SO2=144.5
ppm, SO3=144.5 ppm, H2O=3.16%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6501/T s.
’SO3’ (NH3/NO = 1.06) - NH3=392 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%, SO3=289 ppm,
H2O=3.16%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6501/T s.
T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
Another assumption used during the simulations, is that the decomposition of ammonium
sulfate occurs instantaneously. The gas residence time has been calculated by the dimen-
sions of the reactor hot zone and the volumetric gas ﬂow. During initial simulations, it
78
==PAGE:91==
4.2
Modelling results
has been assumed as a simpliﬁcation, that the ammonium sulfate has been decomposed
into gaseous species, NH3, SO2 etc., upon entering the reactor. However, in reality the
decomposition process will begin upon entering the reactor hot zone. The validity of this
simpliﬁcation has been tested by implementing a combination of the ammonium sulfate
decomposition kinetics found by Wu et al. [47] and the ammonium sulfate decomposition
reaction proposed by Halstead [46] to the model. The decomposition reaction (reaction
R 3.7) has been presented along with the model in section 3.4.1, while the ammonium sul-
fate decomposition kinetics was introduced as part of the model modiﬁcations in section
3.4.2. The NO reduction has then been simulated using ammonium sulfate as an inlet
species, which will decompose to NH3, SO2 etc., relative to initial simulations having NH3
and SO2 as inlet species. In this way, the time of ammonium sulfate decomposition is
considered as part of the total residence time in the reactor hot zone. A comparison of the
results considering the time of decomposition and the initial results neglecting decompo-
sition time is shown in Figure 43. The results are almost identical. Thus, the assumption
is fully valid considering the conditions of this project.
Figure 43: NO reduction in low O2 conditions using model simulation considering the
time of ammonium sulfate decomposition and neglecting the time of ammonium sulfate
decomposition. Average initial conditions for the diﬀerent simulations were:
’Decomposition time neglected’ - NH3=381 ppm, NO=370 ppm, O2=1.25%, SO2=286
ppm H2O=4.04%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6432/T s.
’Decomposition time considered’ - (NH4)2SO4=286 ppm, NO=370 ppm, O2=1.25%,
H2O=4.04%, NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6432/T s.
T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
Other assumptions of importance is the assumption, that the reactor behaves like an
79
==PAGE:92==
4.2
Modelling results
ideal PFR and that the droplet evaporation and particle heating is instantaneous. The
gas ﬂow used during the experiments was relatively low (∼0.5 NL/min) and the reactor
was relatively wide (inner diameter of 2.3 cm). Therefore, it seems valid to assume that
the reactor resembles an ideal PFR. From the literature study, it was found that the
mixing of reducing agent and NO is of great importance. However, it was also found that
the mixing in laboratory-scale setups is very eﬃcient. As the setup used for experiments
of this project is in laboratory-scale, this indicates that the assumption of eﬃcient mixing
is valid. However, it could be interesting to study the mixing in the system by performing
a CFD evaluation, in order to ensure that the mixing is eﬃcient.
It is assumed, that the droplets produced from the mist generator is micron-sized. This
assumption is diﬃcult to verify. However, it is described in the working principle of such
ultrasonic humidiﬁers, that they produce droplets of this size. The evaporation of such
droplets would be more or less instantaneous.
Furthermore, the resulting ammonium
sulfate particles would be signiﬁcantly smaller than the droplets, as the droplets contain
a maximum of 10wt% ammonium sulfate (the case of high AS/NO). The heating of such
small particles would be very rapid. As a long residence time (above 5 s) is available for
all experiments of this project, droplet evaporation and particle heating will only how a
very minor eﬀect on the reduction, if the assumption of micron-sized droplets are valid.
Comparison with literature
The model has also been compared with experimental data from a laboratory-scale jet-
stirred reactor setup by Dagaut and Nicolle [5]. The model simulation has been performed
as a CSTR.
Figure 44 shows the modelling results in the temperature range of 1050 −1500 K, which
has been compared to experimental results in the range of 1100 −1450 K. The mod-
elling results show a temperature window, which begins at around 1100 K without SO2
and approximately 1150 K with SO2. The optimum temperature with and without SO2 is
approximately 1210 K and 1180 K respectively for the modelling results. At these temper-
atures NOﬁnal/NOinitial = 0.33 both with and without SO2. The experimental results with
SO2 has an optimum temperature of approximately 1280 K with NOﬁnal/NOinitial ≈0.34,
while NOﬁnal/NOinitial ≈0.33 at an optimum temperature of approximately 1250 K with-
out SO2. For the case of the experimental results, the temperature window of eﬀective
NO reduction starts in the range of ∼1180 −1210 K. At temperatures higher than the
optimum, the reduction eﬃciency is decreasing relatively slowly with increasing temper-
atures. Above the optimum temperature, the reduction is lower for the modelling results
with no SO2, compared to the modelling results with SO2 and the experimental results,
which all have similar reduction.
80
==PAGE:93==
4.2
Modelling results
Figure 44: Comparison of initial model and experimental data adapted from the literature
[5]. Initial conditions: NO=500 ppm, NH3=500 ppm, O2=6250 ppm and SO2=1000 ppm
(black) or SO2=0 ppm (blue). Residence time of 100 ms.
From Figure 44 it is seen, that the maximum reduction predicted by the model conform
to that of the experimental data of Dagaut and Nicolle [5], both in the case with and
without SO2. However, the model predicts that the temperature window is located at
lower temperatures than observed from the experimental data. Both the model and the
experimental results show, that the introduction of 1000 ppm SO2 shifts the temperature
window towards higher temperatures. Both below and above the optimum temperature,
the eﬀect of SO2 is larger for the modelling results relative to the experimental results.
However, the optimum is shifted approximately 30 K towards higher temperatures by the
introduction of 1000 ppm SO2 for both the modelling and experimental results.
4.2.2
Modiﬁed model
In this section, it is investigated how the model modiﬁcations introduced in section 3.4.2
have aﬀected the model performance.
Introduction of NH2SO2 subset
Initially, it is tested how the addition of the NH2SO2 subset has aﬀected the performance
of the model. Results using the initial model and the model with the NH2SO2 addition
(without modiﬁcation of the ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction) are compared in
Figure 45.
81
==PAGE:94==
4.2
Modelling results
Figure 45: Comparison of initial model and modiﬁed model with NH2SO2 subset included.
Initial conditions: NH3=392 ppm, NO=371 ppm, O2=1.26%, SO2=289 ppm, H2O=3.16%,
NO2=28.8 ppm and residence time of 6501/T s.
From Figure 45 it is seen, that the introduction of the additional subset has not had any
noticeable eﬀect on the model performance. This indicate that the NH2SO2 is not a stable
species at the temperatures investigated during this project, and the reaction sequence
will therefore not proceed to an extent, which will aﬀect the NO reduction.
Alternative ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction
In the initial model, it was assumed that all sulfur was released as SO2 during thermal
decomposition of ammonium sulfate as proposed by Halstead [46]. However, Wu et al.
suggested that the sulfur was released as a mixture of SO2 and SO3 during thermal
decomposition of ammonium sulfate. An alternative ammonium sulfate decomposition
reaction scheme has been proposed during this project, which combines features from
both the decomposition reactions of Halstead [46] and Wu et al. [47]. The alternative
ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction scheme has been presented in section 3.4.2.
No more modiﬁcations have been made during this project.
Thus, the eﬀects of the
alternative ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction scheme has been investigated by
comparing the ﬁnal model to the initial model and the experimental results in section
4.2.3.
4.2.3
Final model
The complete ﬁnal model can be found in Appendix 7.6. In this section, the ﬁnal model
has been compared to the initial model and the experimental results.
