Model analysis
The ﬁnal model has been analyzed. Sensitivity analysis and reaction path analysis have
been performed for the case of medium AS with both low and high O2. In addition,
the analyses have been performed at two diﬀerent temperatures for both cases of O2
concentration; one in the beginning of the temperature window for eﬀective reduction
and one at the optimum temperature. All analyses have been performed at a time of
approximately 20% NO conversion.
Figure 52 shows a sensitivity analysis for NO for initial molar ratio of AS/NO = 0.77
and O2=1.25%. The temperature of 1083 K is slightly above the onset temperature for
the process, while the temperature of 1133 K is the optimum temperature. It is seen
90
==PAGE:103==
4.2
Modelling results
that the NO reduction is most sensitive towards the chain branching reaction, reaction
R 2.21 (NH2 + NO ⇌NNH + OH), which enhances the NO reduction. On the other
hand, terminating reaction R 2.22, NH2 + NO ⇌N2 + H2O, is similarly unfavorable, as it
inhibits NO reduction. These two reactions have by far the largest sensitivity coeﬃcients.
Furthermore, reaction R 2.24 (H+O2 ⇌O+OH), which is also chain branching, enhances
NO reduction. However, this reaction has a much lower sensitivity coeﬃcient. The rest
of the reactions are of less importance, due to their lower sensitivity coeﬃcients.
Figure 52: Most signiﬁcant sensitivity coeﬃcients for prediction of NO in the model in
medium AS and low O2 conditions. Average initial conditions for the experiments were:
(NH4)2SO4=286 ppm, NO=370 ppm, O2=1.25%, H2O=4.04%, NO2=28.8 ppm and resi-
dence time of 6432/T s. T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
Similarly, Figure 53 shows a sensitivity analysis for NO for initial molar ratio of AS/NO =
0.77 and O2=8.09%. Here, the temperature of 1023 K is slightly above the onset tem-
perature for the process, while the temperature of 1073 K is the optimum temperature.
Again, the reduction enhancing reaction R 2.21 (NH2 +NO ⇌NNH+OH) and reduction
inhibiting reaction R 2.22 (NH2 + NO ⇌N2 + H2O) have by far the largest sensitivity
coeﬃcients, followed by the reduction enhancing reaction R 2.24 (H+O2 ⇌O+OH) with
signiﬁcantly lower sensitivity coeﬃcient. Furthermore, NH2 + NO2 ⇌H2NO + NO has a
relatively large sensitivity coeﬃcient at the optimum temperature for this case, while the
rest of the reactions have relatively low sensitivity coeﬃcients.
91
==PAGE:104==
4.2
Modelling results
Figure 53: Most signiﬁcant sensitivity coeﬃcients for prediction of NO in the model in
medium AS and high O2 conditions. Average initial conditions for the experiments were:
(NH4)2SO4=278 ppm, NO=359 ppm, O2=8.09%, H2O=3.93%, NO2=40.4 ppm and resi-
dence time of 6446/T s. T is inserted in K. Balance is N2.
The general trend for both cases of oxygen concentration is that the sensitivity coeﬃcients
are larger at the optimum temperature relative to the lower temperature at the onset for
the process. However, for both cases, the reaction of NH2+HONO ⇌NH3+NO2 deviates
from the trend.
From an analysis of the reaction paths, the most important paths of the process have
been sketched up in a pathway diagram.
Figure 54 shows a pathway diagram of the
nitrogen chemistry of the process, while Figure 55 and Figure 56 show pathway diagrams
of the sulfur chemistry of the process in low (1.25%) and high (8.09%) oxygen conditions
respectively.
From the pathway diagram of the nitrogen chemistry of the process, seen in Figure 54,
it is seen that initially ammonium sulfate is decomposed to NH3, which is converted to
NH2 by reaction with the O/H radical pool, primarily with OH through reaction R 2.20.
Subsequently, NH2 and NO reacts via reactions R 2.21 (NH2 + NO ⇌NNH + OH) and
R 2.22 (NH2 + NO ⇌N2 + H2O).
These reactions are the key step of the process,
and as seen from the sensitivity analysis also the reactions with the largest sensitivity
coeﬃcients. As illustrated by dashed lines in Figure 54, NH2 reacts with NO2 as well, but
to a smaller extent. This leads to a less signiﬁcant pathway, which produces some NO.
This NO is again consumed by reaction with NH2, as described in the main pathway. The
pathway diagram is valid for both oxygen concentrations around 1.25% and 8.09%, and
at temperatures slightly above the onset temperature for the process and at the optimum
temperature.
92
==PAGE:105==
4.2
Modelling results
Figure 54: Reaction path diagram for NO reduction with ammonium sulfate. Dashed lines
denote a pathway less signiﬁcant than the fully drawn lines, but still relatively important.
Red lines and species have been described in the major path.
The main paths of the sulfur chemistry of the process, at an oxygen concentration of
1.25%, are depicted in Figure 55. Initially, the sulfur from ammonium sulfate is converted
to SO2 and SO3 during decomposition. The SO3 is subsequently consumed by reaction,
either with H and NO to form SO2 or with HO2 to form HOSO2. The reaction between
SO3 and NO is more signiﬁcant at lower temperatures. The HOSO2 is ﬁnally converted
to SO2.
93
==PAGE:106==
4.2
Modelling results
Figure 55: Reaction path diagram for the sulfur chemistry during the NO reduction with
ammonium sulfate at low oxygen concentration (1.25%).
Similarly, the main paths of the sulfur chemistry of the process are depicted in Figure 56,
but at an oxygen concentration of 8.09%. SO2 and SO3 are formed during decomposition
of ammonium sulfate, after which SO3 is consumed by reaction with either H to form SO2
or with HO2 to form HOSO2. At this high oxygen concentration, SO2 reacts with NO2
and forms SO3, especially at higher temperatures. The HOSO2 is converted to SO2.
Figure 56: Reaction path diagram for the sulfur chemistry during the NO reduction
with ammonium sulfate at high oxygen concentration (8.09%). Dashed lines denote less
signiﬁcant reactions.
At low oxygen concentrations, the main consumption of SO3 results from the reaction with
H, while the reaction with HO2 is the main consumption path for SO3 at high oxygen
concentrations.
94
==PAGE:107==
4.2
Modelling results
Model challenges
Comparing the initial model and the ﬁnal model with the experimental results, it is seen
that the ﬁnal model performance is worse than the initial model performance. One of the
expected main reasons is the amount of NH3 in the system. It is assumed, that even in the
initial model the amount of NH3 in the system after ammonium sulfate decomposition is
overestimated. This is partly caused by expected loss of ammonium sulfate by adsorption
to the glass-ware of the reactor top piece. This cannot be adjusted in the model and
have to be ﬁxed by modifying the feeding conﬁguration in order to prevent adsorption.
However, it is furthermore expected based on the results of this project and unpublished
studies of Krum [67], that the yield of NH3 from ammonium sulfate decomposition has
been overestimated. In order to take into account the expected formation of SO3 in the
ﬁnal model, an ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction suggesting an even higher NH3
yield than initially, had to be considered. This resulted in worsened performance of the
ﬁnal model relative to the initial model compared to experimental results. Thus, in order
to improve the model a better understanding of the thermal decomposition of ammonium
sulfate reaction and its products at the experimental conditions is needed. More detailed
studies of the NH3, SO2 and SO3 yields from ammonium sulfate decomposition could help
further improvement of the model.
Rasmussen et al. [65] describes the removal of radicals by SO2. Thus, the presence of
SO2 reduces the radical pool, which could be an explanation for the inhibiting eﬀect of
SO2 on the NO reduction, as the radical pool is of great importance in the reduction
of NO. However, the modelling results of this project indicate, that the model does not
adequately predict the radical removal in the temperature range investigated during this
project.
95
==PAGE:108==
5
Conclusion
During this project the selective non-catalytic reduction of NO by ammonium sulfate has
been investigated. The ammonium sulfate has been introduced in an aqueous ammonium
sulfate solution. Initially, an experimental setup capable of feeding micron-sized droplets
to a homogeneous ﬂow reactor has been developed. The setup has been used to exper-
imentally investigate the reduction of NO, including the eﬀects of varying the oxygen
level and the initial ratio of AS/NO. Furthermore, the results of reduction by ammonium
sulfate have been compared to gas reference experiments using NH3 and SO2. These gas
reference experiments have also been used to investigate the eﬀect of SO2 on the reduc-
tion of NO. Finally, the experimental results have been compared to an initial chemical
kinetics model and a modiﬁed chemical kinetics model.
From the experimental results of NO reduction using ammonium sulfate as reducing agent,
it can be concluded that the NO reduction eﬃciency increases by an increase in the initial
ratio of AS/NO, while the optimum temperature is not signiﬁcantly changed. Further-
more, it was found that the maximum reduction is improved by lowering the oxygen
concentration in the system. An additional eﬀect of lowering the oxygen concentration
is that the temperature window is shifted towards higher temperatures. The NO2 outlet
concentration generally decreases with increasing temperatures and with lowering of the
oxygen concentration of the system. No clear distinction is seen in the NO2 outlet concen-
tration from the low to medium NH3 conditions, while the concentration is signiﬁcantly
lower at high NH3 conditions.
From results obtained using reference gas experiments, it can be concluded that SO2 has
an inhibiting eﬀect on NO reduction at the conditions studied during this project. The
location of the temperature window is not signiﬁcantly changed. However, the maximum
reduction of NO is signiﬁcantly lower when SO2 is introduced to the system.
The reduction of NO by ammonium sulfate is signiﬁcantly less eﬃcient than by reference
gas conditions with, estimated from literature decomposition data, similar amounts of
ammonia. From other studies in the literature it is seen that SO3 might be an additional
product of ammonium sulfate decomposition and SO3 might have a more pronounced
inhibiting eﬀect relative to SO2. Furthermore, it was observed that some ammonium
sulfate is adsorbed to the glass-ware before entering the reactor. Thus, the amount of
NH3 available for reduction is slightly lower than expected from calculations.
The initial model simulations show an overestimation of the NO reduction relative to the
experimental results. For the gas reference experiment without SO2, the model predictions
are satisfactory. Thus, it can be concluded that the model performance is good in the
absence of sulfur compounds and with NH3 as reducing agent. However, the inhibiting
eﬀect of SO2/SO3 cannot be predicted satisfactorily by the model. This is seen for both
the gas reference experiment with SO2 and for the ammonium sulfate experiments.
The ﬁnal model performs worse than the initial model, due to a higher NH3 yield from the
modiﬁed ammonium sulfate decomposition reaction. The higher amount of NH3 results in
96
==PAGE:109==
even better NO reduction around the optimum. As the predicted reduction of the initial
model was already signiﬁcantly higher than seen from the experimental results, this has
impaired the model regarding the maximum NO reduction. No clear conclusions can be
made for the eﬀect of the model modiﬁcations on the location of the temperature window.
From the model analysis, it is seen that the reaction between NH2 and NO is the key
step of the process. The reaction has two channels, and it is found from the sensitivity
analysis, that these reactions have the largest sensitivity coeﬃcients. Furthermore, it is
seen from an analysis of the reaction paths, that these reactions are part of the major
reaction path for the process.
The prospects of using ammonium sulfate as reducing agent for industrial purposes have
been impaired by the results of this project, as the performance of the reduction has
been signiﬁcantly worse compared to results using ammonia. From the results of this
project, it can be concluded that a signiﬁcantly higher amount of ammonium sulfate has
to be used relative to ammonia in order to achieve similar NO reduction. However, for
industrial purposes the inhibiting species SO2 and SO3 would be continuously consumed
by sulfation of potassium. Other diﬀerences of the conditions between the laboratory-
scale experiments performed during this project and industrial purposes may aﬀect the
reduction as well.
Thus, a wider range of experiments, investigating the inﬂuence of
other changes of the conditions, have to be performed to evaluate the potential use of
ammonium sulfate as an alternative reducing agent.
6
Future work
The reviewed literature suggests that the main products of thermal decomposition of am-
monium sulfate is NH3, SO2 and/or SO3. However, the exact decomposition process and
products diﬀer for the diﬀerent studies. Therefore, it was desired to experimentally inves-
tigate the thermal decomposition of ammonium sulfate, at the experimental conditions
used during this project. However, due to challenges with loss of NH3, SO2 and/or SO3,
which is assumed to occur downstream the reactor, neither qualitative nor quantitative
conclusions of the decomposition products can be made. Therefore, it would be inter-
esting to experimentally investigate the thermal decomposition process and its products
in conditions similar to the experimental conditions of this project.
Hereby, a better
understanding of the initial reduction conditions could be achieved.
Furthermore, it would be interesting to investigate a larger variety of oxygen concentra-
tions and initial NH3/NO molar ratios to conﬁrm the trends found during this project.
It would be interesting to further develop and improve the feeding system of the mist, in
order to obtain a more stable feeding rate and to ensure that all ammonium sulfate is fed
to the reactor and not adsorbed to the glass-ware.
In order to test the KCl reducing properties of ammonium sulfate, it would be interesting
to also add KCl to a system similar to that used in this project.
97
==PAGE:110==
In order to evaluate the assumption of ideal mixing between gases in the reactor, it would
be interesting to perform a CFD study of the reactor. Subsequently, experiments could
be performed to test the results of the CFD study.
Finally, the model needs to be further improved in order more accurately capture the
inhibiting eﬀect of SO2.
98
